Q83443-Everyday_Miracles-Ebook-21

A

Chapter 6

To Bee in the Heavenly Procession

Floral Cora

Debbie told Cora she was too afraid of dying to fly anywhere, let alone a long-haul flight from California to Europe. Why her practical exercise partner was not at all hesitant to fly all the way to France seemed illogically mismatched to her typical sensible thinking. Cora was not crossing the Atlantic for a compelling crisis. She was only going to Lourdes for her annual volunteer service. Actually, Cora was accustomed to lengthy flights, having immigrated years earlier from the Philippines to the United States. She neither liked nor disliked spending hours on a plane to visit her family every January or to go anywhere at any time. It was just that she had a peace about dying and air travel that did not come from statistics or expressions intended to comfort reluctant travelers, like “we are much safer in a plane than in a car.” Cora was not fearless or fearful of flying. She was just a woman confidently grounded in faith.

Cora explained that God could take her home to Himself whenever He wanted; she was not afraid to die. “Are your bags packed?” Debbie asked, rolling her eyes. Cora thoughtfully self-checked whether her soul was as prepared to meet God as her suitcases were ready for international travel. “Yes! My white nurse volunteer uniforms are folded and packed in my luggage. I’m ready to go to Lourdes or to go with God. I am really ready — right now!” Cora smiled as Debbie shrugged off the spiritual sentiment. They finished up in the locker room and walked outside together. “See you next time,” they said, without thought, walking to their cars.

Cora returned home to prune her roses in the pleasant morning sun. Michael and Cora tended impressive flower beds in both the back yard of their house and across their entire front yard. Coming from the scenic island of Cebu, Cora cherished the flowers and lush greenery typical of the tropical gardens back home. Living in California, she nurtured floral delights with delicate scents interspersed amidst winding stone pathways and outdoor statues. Michael was born in Bombay while his father was heading up an American bank expansion. He was an equally enthusiastic garden hobbyist with extensive plantings reminiscent of his childhood in fragrant India and his teen years spent living abroad in Lebanon. The couple equally enjoyed time with each other and with their extensive gardens. Cora returned from the gym intending to prune the roses nearest the walkway by the front door. This task could be completed in time to depart for noon Mass and to escape the crescendo of California’s midday rising heat.

The bee stung Cora on her neck at about eleven forty-five that morning. It was unpleasant, but not extremely painful. She had only been stung by a bee once before. It had swollen quite badly, but Cora did not know that severe swelling was a potential indication of a bee sting allergy. She felt dizzy immediately. She was puzzled why she felt so strange, but made no connection between the sting and her escalating wooziness. Instead of going inside to freshen up, grab the keys, and drive to church in her usual daily routine, Cora barely made her way inside the house. Noticing the familiar daily show airing on the large television screen as she entered the living area, Cora knew it was after eleven thirty, but before noon. She thought maybe she was hungry or thirsty — she knew something was very wrong. It was as if she were swaying on the outside and floating on the inside. She immediately began to pray the Rosary, s-l-o-w-l-y. Cora was dangerously close to dying. She felt as though time had inexplicably been suspended, slowed to almost still, as if every second was mysteriously prolonged, inexplicably, somehow.

The Holy Rosary is a scriptural form of prayer favored for centuries by saints and everyday faithful alike. This powerful intercessory prayer has been a meditative practice for deepening a relationship with Our Lord by contemplating significant moments throughout His life and ministry. Rosary beads are a practical, holy tool to help a person focus on contemplative meditation and to pray undistracted by the counting of individual prayers. Made of glass, stone, wood, knotted rope, or glow-in-the-dark plastic for children, the larger beads are for the Our Father prayer with sets of ten smaller beads for successive Hail Mary prayers. Recently canonized Pope St. Paul VI was known to say that, when praying the Rosary, key moments of the Gospel can be experienced. Cora knew that and had always loved to pray the Rosary; it was her favorite prayer.

Cora lay helplessly on the floor, motionless, her rosary beads clenched in her fist. Suddenly, she remembered her earlier comment in the ladies’ locker room at the gym. She pleaded with the Blessed Mother, crying to her as a regretful child would to an understanding mom, “Please forgive me, I’m not ready to die. I want to be here for my son returning home from Iraq — and I need to go to Lourdes and volunteer for you.” Cora wept softly while pleading with the Mother of God to help her. Then, it was as if time remained inexplicably stretched in a mystical way, allowing her to continue her prayers.

Michael did not often have time to talk to his wife during his busy workdays. Neither of their schedules was routine or predictable, especially when Mike was working a job in San Francisco proper. Traffic could easily derail his day, often delaying his drive and arrival home. Cora called as Michael was nearing the Golden Gate Bridge. Her speech sounded slurred. Worried, he called her back. No answer. He called two neighbors. He was unable to reach them. Mike finally dialed 9-1-1. It was just after four o’clock in the afternoon.

Cora had left the door unlocked as she came in from the garden. The neighbors had a key, but it was not needed. They entered with the paramedics to find Cora on the floor — eerily still. She was unnaturally blue. Cora was barely breathing and cold to the touch. The ambulance crew ultimately discovered her swollen neck and found the bee sting. The emergency operator instructed Mike not to drive home, but to instead drive directly to the hospital. They told him there was no need to rush and cautioned him to drive slowly. No mention was made that Cora might not be alive. She arrived unresponsive. She was treated for a severe allergic reaction and admitted to the intensive care unit. Cora was in a coma. Mike was told that her prognosis was not promising. She was loved by many, and her faithful family and friends stormed Heaven, pleading in prayer for her life.

Cora remained unconscious the day after the bee sting. Unaware of her coma, Cora instead found herself in Lourdes, France. She was somehow looking from up above at the pilgrims that were beneath her in the Grotto at Lourdes. They were preparing for the daily Eucharistic Procession. She could clearly see the top of the head of each person as they turned to cross over the bridge to be near the outdoor altar. Cora followed them, joining them by floating above. She could see and sense the excitement mixed with reverent anticipation of the Blessed Sacrament’s arrival to commence the regal procession. Crowds and small groups were converging with stragglers while the St. Joseph’s volunteer men hurriedly attempted to maintain an orderly queue in preparation for a dignified procession. Cora could see the volunteers had their hands full! Under the expert guidance of the St. Joseph Brancardiers (volunteer stretcher bearers), everything and everyone eventually fell into place, as it somehow amazingly always does every day at Lourdes.

The Eucharistic Procession is a highlight of the daily pilgrimage for many in Lourdes and not to be missed. The trumpets blow in evangelical harmony, with a muted bugle sounding a royal announcement: Our Lord is coming! Catholics believe Jesus Christ is truly present in the Holy Eucharist, the Blessed Sacrament (see Luke 22:19). The sick join in at the place of privilege at the very front of the procession, followed by the Blessed Sacrament under a four-corner poled canopy held by volunteers dressed in respectful uniforms. Banners precede the holy fanfare worthy of Christ the Risen King. For some, the daily Eucharistic Procession at Lourdes might be the first or only time to witness this magnificent historic devotional.

A procession is a holy follow-the-way proceeding and should not be confused with the pomp of a parade. Every day at Lourdes, between Holy Week and All Saints’ Day, tens of thousands process with the Holy Eucharist from across the Grotto, over the bridge along the Basilica ramps onto the Esplanade roadway to follow Our Lord into the St. Pius X Underground Basilica. Once inside, with up to twenty-five thousand pilgrims, and sometimes more, hundreds of priests process to the altar. The music continues with an impromptu group of volunteers from around the globe, incredibly sounding like a well-rehearsed, world-class professional choir. It is an astounding beautiful, and incomparable holy event. Even though she was in a deep coma, Cora experienced the procession with fervent joy. Enthralled, she followed the Holy Eucharist, continuing from above, a perfect vantage point for sound and sight without the usual obstruction of the crowds down on the ground. Cora was filled with awe, content to follow Jesus to the happiness of the other world. In these moments she did not realize her body was in a hospital bed in California, far away from Lourdes, France.

On the third day of her life-suspending coma, Cora’s Lourdes Volunteers friends brought Lourdes Water to bless her. She soon surfaced from her deep sleep in the intensive care unit. Cora excitedly insisted that she call the office of Lourdes Volunteers. She exclaimed in a strained, groggy voice that she wanted to explain that when we go from this world to the other world, we just have to follow Jesus in the Holy Eucharist to Heaven! It’s the same way we process at Lourdes. This must be what happens when we die! It’s a holy road map — just follow Jesus in the Blessed Sacrament! Cora said if everyone just understood, if they would only go to Lourdes, they would know the way to Heaven. Simply follow Jesus in the Holy Eucharist in the Eucharistic procession because “He is the way!” (see John 14:6).

Cora fell back into a coma for a few more days, but woke up later to fully recover. The physicians said it was impossible for someone with such a severe allergic reaction to remain alive for hours after a deadly bee sting. The doctor was sure Cora was mistaken about the time she was stung. Cora is certain it was about eleven forty-five in the morning, the time she usually left for daily Mass. She knew because of the specific Catholic program streaming on her television when she came inside immediately after the bee sting.

What Cora could not explain is why it took her more than four hours to pray one Rosary, which usually takes only about fifteen to twenty minutes to pray. She said, “It was as if God slowed or suspended time while I prayed the Rosary until help could arrive hours later to save my life.”

Cora is confident the Mother of God heard her heartfelt request, remaining with Cora and interceding for her throughout her prayerful plea to welcome her only son back from deployment. Cora is forever grateful for this favor and insists that the Blessed Mother is the best Heavenly friend to ask to pray for us when we are most in need of a seemingly impossible favor!

Grant us your tender devotion to Mary, the Mother of Jesus and our Mother. Accompany us on our earthly pilgrimage toward the blessed homeland, where we hope to arrive to contemplate forever the glory of the Father, the Son and the Holy Spirit. Amen. (Pope St. John Paul II)

Cora in Her Rose Garden

Cora returned to Lourdes to make her commitment to serve in the Piscines (baths) in St. Jean Baptiste Service as a North American Lourdes Volunteer in 2010. She hand-crafts and gifts hundreds of crystal rosary beads each year in thanksgiving for her “time-suspending, life-saving Rosary” prayer and frequently hosts prayer groups in both California and Cebu.
Q83443-Everyday_Miracles-Ebook-22
Q83443-Everyday_Miracles-Ebook-23

A

Chapter 7

A Sign of the Cross

Joyful Jamie

Jamie was bitter. He wasn’t born that way — he was born with cerebral palsy. Jamie came into the world good-natured. Turning from teen to young adult, he slowly stewed himself into sour bitterness. Resentful was his upgrade from discouraged after he left school and no longer needed to be somewhere and with others every day. God was not present in Jamie’s life, or so he thought. Religion was only for his sister. After their parents divorced and went their separate ways, his sister found a God-centered life. Jamie moved into a group home and later claimed his quadriplegic adulthood in an act of bold independence by moving in with a quad friend.

Both men required full care provided by social services through governmental agencies. His roommate had a girlfriend and was financially supported by his family, who provided him with a condominium and supplemental private care. Jamie was totally dependent upon government services. Otherwise, he was mostly on his own in this unusual living arrangement. Once the seeds of bitterness sprouted, they grew in Jamie like prickly weeds, deepening their ugly, expansive roots.

His only sister knew God was the answer. She was praying for Jamie’s conversion, for him to turn to God and to be at peace with his physical limitations in his adult life. She was hoping for sooner rather than later, before his dwindling natural goodness was totally snuffed out. If she could just get him to Lourdes — the holy place of miracles and healing — she was confident the graces would overtake his bitterness to restore and renew Jamie. She wasn’t praying for him to jump out of his wheelchair or suddenly use his arms or legs; she was praying for his soul. The body is temporary, but the soul is forever. And as the old saying goes, in the end — nothing matters but the state of the soul. Jamie’s sister enlisted a small but powerful army of saintly religious sisters to take on praying specifically for her brother. If Hell hath no fury like a woman scorned, then Heaven has no joy like cloistered nuns praying for the conversion of a lost soul! Jamie didn’t stand a chance! Those persistent nuns were going to root the anger out of him prayerfully, lovingly, and quickly, so he could be reformed in faith to flourish in God’s infinite love for him.

The trip to Lourdes was a near disaster. It might have been a sparkling new millennium of hope and a few new decades into handicapped accessibility in both France and the United States, but accessibility advancements were mostly stationary and on the ground. The airlines did not anticipate the necessity for serious special needs adaptability in economy class. That was going to take another decade. Very few quadriplegic customers flew on long-haul international flights. If they did, those passengers were usually flying in upgraded business or first class and could afford the pricy airfare. Getting Jamie onto the plane, down the narrow aisle and into an economy seat accessible to the non-handicapped restroom, with dignity and without incident, was a challenge. Every humiliating frustration along the way could have soured anyone, but for love. Love made the difference and, ultimately, the transformation. Each obstacle to getting Jamie to Lourdes and back was conquered over time, often at thirty-five thousand feet while flying five hundred miles per hour. Both volunteers who traveled with Jamie and airline personnel on the flights literally rose to the occasion. The biblical sentiment “love never ends” (1 Cor. 13:8) proved to be true, both in real time and in real life.

His complaining and criticizing while on the four flights to Lourdes, although justifiable and for valid reasons, revealed the depth of the well of self-pity and anger within Jamie. He was questioning his purpose in life, but his thoughtful pondering was soon to be answered. Jamie was unknowingly about to become a significant catechist as a fruit of his soon-to-be conversion. God could have chosen anyone; God chose Jamie.

Jamie was cared for in Lourdes by people who paid for their own pilgrimage dedicated to helping him. Back at home, hired staff earned an honorable paycheck to professionally care for Jamie. Each volunteer in Lourdes literally and physically became the selfless hands and feet of Jesus Christ to Jamie. Embraced in the warmth of this authentic, loving care under the gaze of the Mother of God in the Grotto, Jamie was inspired to return to his childhood Faith.

Like Bernadette, after his first visit to the Grotto, Jamie went to Confession. After another visit to the Grotto, like Bernadette before the last apparition, Jamie received the Holy Eucharist. Every encounter with the light of Christ in each person sparked a light within Jamie to illuminate his heart, leaving no room for dark anger or blackened bitterness. Each volunteer, nurse, doctor, pilgrim, and priest genuinely loved Jamie. This love expanded to overpower all the darkness that had consumed him. “One person to another” is how Bernadette described her encounter with the most beautiful lady in the Grotto. One person to another was the love that forever changed Jamie’s heart and his life. The cloistered nuns who had been praying unceasingly won! Jamie was transformed in faith.

Back at the airport, Jamie knew the handicapped accessibility of the airlines had not changed in one week. The difficulties he experienced on the return flights were similar to those on the outbound travel. It was how Jamie responded that was so drastically different.

Aisle chairs are armless mini wheelchair-like contraptions. They are designed to fit exactly between the narrow rows of aircraft seats to move down the aisles. It is a tight squeeze. Any protruding elbows are exposed to painful banging on stiff metal armrests. A passenger is lifted from their personal wheelchair, put into an airport wheelchair, lifted again onto an aisle chair, and strapped in to enter and exit the aircraft. Throw in a language barrier with few such encounters and the infrequent inexperience can range from precarious to almost comical contortions.

On the return flight, the aisle chair was missing from the aircraft. It was either hidden somewhere under the belly of the plane, inside a cabin recess, or possibly inadvertently left at the airport where it was last used. To the credit of the airlines, and Air France specifically, sweeping changes were made in a few short years through increasing awareness and the kind yet passionate persistence of Lourdes Volunteers and compassionate Air France personnel. Meetings and on-the-fly friendly negotiations with dedicated ground and flight crews consistently came together for needed changes to develop quickly over a relatively short time. But that was a few years yet to come. Jamie and others had to first endure a few years of turbulent exchanges necessitating the heightened attention of one of the world’s largest airlines.

Jamie’s return flight was held awaiting an aisle chair to conduct him safely onto the plane. Passengers, the pilot, and the crew were all increasingly anxious about missing their connecting international flights in Paris. The flight needed to take off without further delay. Leaving Jamie behind was not an option. The pilgrimage leader was pleading with the airline to negotiate a dignified solution to honor boarding for a special needs passenger. The longer the delay, the more the distress mounted. Jamie finally agreed to be carried onto the aircraft, like a baby, in the arms of his adult male caregiver. Jamie could have felt humiliated, insulted, or justifiably angry. Instead, Jamie was beaming, smiling ear-to-ear! The full plane errupted into spontaneous applause! It was hard to complain about the seriousness of this situation when the wronged passenger was so unmistakably happy.

Remarkably, his joyful embrace spurred airline personnel to commit to making the changes needed to welcome Jamie and the other special needs passengers on future flights with fewer incidents. And there would be many more to come. Airlines needed to accommodate this unexpected new niche market of passengers with varied and serious disabilities, previously mostly unable or reluctant to travel to France without a Lourdes Hospitality. Love transformed Jamie — and, contagiously, the airline personnel he encountered. The grace flowed beyond the Grotto spring in a ripple effect.

Some struggles continued for some flights, but love overcame every obstacle each time. It is said, where there is love, there is suffering. For this arduous travel, it became clear that where there was suffering, there needed to be love. Moved by Jamie’s conversion, a sponsor offered to fund him to return to Lourdes annually. Like gas filling a tank about to run empty, Jamie returned to Lourdes the next year to be filled with the grace of the Grotto for another year.

On each return pilgrimage, Jamie would learn more to be able to teach more. During the initial encounter between Bernadette and Our Lady in the Grotto, the first action of the two young girls was to pray, making the Sign of the Cross. Bernadette knew when the Lady lifted the crucifix of her rosary beads to her forehead that the Lady was thinking of the God Who created them. Bernadette said later in her short life, “If we make this one prayer well, we can go to Heaven.”

Like a holy shield, we cover ourselves with the God Who created us, the Son Who died to save us, and the Holy Spirit Who breathes life into our faith. This motion of our arm from our mind to the center of our being, and then from our left shoulder across our hearts to our right shoulder, is a physical act of faith. This simple prayer covers us with the depth and breadth of the Holy Cross. Many of us are in the good habit of making the Sign of the Cross, but sometimes it slips into a habitual gesture, like the thing we do before and after we pray grace, join in a formal prayer, or bless ourselves with holy water. Sometimes, we are not mindful of the Trinity or of authentic prayer. Rather, we lapse into a quick and thoughtless circular motion, more like the Heavenly eternity we desire than the Cross that makes our eternal entrance possible.

The genuine prayer of the Sign of the Cross profoundly struck Jamie with the example of Bernadette following the Immaculate Conception. In the Accueil Marie Saint-Frai, Jamie confided to his pilgrimage leader that he wished he could use his arm, if only once. He wanted to make the Sign of the Cross like Bernadette, like the Mother of God. When the group prayed, he imagined he would make the motions with them. Jamie could have wished to move a limb for so many other reasons that most of us take for granted in our mobile daily activities. Jamie could not scratch an itch, brush his teeth, open a door, wipe away a tear, or sign his name. Yet, if he could move his arm — only once — it would be to make the Sign of the Cross.

The pilgrimage leader listened intently and tearfully. How many times had she made a poor Sign of the Cross with her capable arms before knowing Bernadette and hearing Jamie? How did it go unnoticed that Jamie was motionless while the rest of the pilgrimage group made the movements along with this prayer? Making the Sign of the Cross over Jamie was to become a blessing and catechesis for many. Every time the pilgrimage family prayed together, the pilgrimage leader would discreetly seek out Jamie to physically make the Sign of the Cross over him.

They said Bernadette prayed the Sign of the Cross with all her heart. Jamie prayed the same way, sometimes with someone making the physical gestures for him. Jamie asked the volunteers at meals to make the Sign of the Cross over him before and after grace. They would make the Sign of the Cross over Jamie and then themselves in the same way they would put a forkful of food in his mouth at the table and then lift their fork to take a bite from their own plate of food. This crucial gesture brought the magnitude of this simple yet significant prayer into a deeper depth of meaning for everyone. A profound thankfulness for the ability to pray this prayer freely and with the physical gestures of moving one arm expanded to an awareness of oppressed people who are fearful to be found out as Christians or those who risk their lives should they be caught making the Sign of the Cross. Soon after, everyone was taking this renewed love for this prayer back home with them. Another pilgrimage leader was inspired by this to invite parishes learning about Lourdes to offer a prayerful and reverent Sign of the Cross for someone who could not. Jamie had affirmed his first and primary purpose as a Lourdes Volunteers teacher and catechist in a call to authentic prayer.

Unable to sit, stand, walk, dress, bathe, or reach over to hug someone, Jamie could have remained steeped in resentment. In his stillness, he chose instead to follow Bernadette to the Grotto to learn more. Dying to self was the next lesson to be learned. Bernadette said, “If the Good God allows it, there must be something good in it for me.” Jamie noticed Bernadette called Him the Good God even when life was not always so good for her. Dying to what Jamie wanted or wished to do accumulated into an often nerve-wracking and never-ending pile of daily frustrations. His situation did not change. Again, he transformed by offering to God with joyful surrender each small thing he could not do. The penance of what he was unable to do became his humble, prayerful offering. When he was asked to teach teenage volunteers, an age notorious for selfishness, Jamie invited them to an experiment while serving in Lourdes. Simply be present — smiling. Be the physical feet of Christ because your feet can move. He suggested they wheel a pilgrim around town to shop for rosary beads — without complaint — when going with friends for a French ice cream would taste better. Then, come back to report.

The youth were amazed and returned to tell Jamie they had received more than they had given. They were happier doing for others than if they had done what they originally wanted to do for themselves. They were dying to themselves and their preferences. This allowed the desire of someone else to live through their sacrifice. The more you do this, the better you will be at this, he told them. When you slip up or fail — and you probably will — offer the next opportunity for what someone else wants that you can help them to do instead of what you want. Jamie quickly became the favored catechist for Dying-to-Self Day during the pilgrimage week.

Jamie was generously sponsored every year. He continues to make the annual journey after more than a dozen years of pilgrimage. Jamie considers his pilgrimages to be personal miracles of Lourdes. Once bored and bitter, Jamie became prayerful and joyful. When Jamie was alone or back in the group home, he made the Sign of the Cross with all his heart, imagining the gestures over him. When he is on pilgrimage, a new group of sloppy sign makers always arrives to unexpectedly learn from Jamie how to make the Sign of the Cross well, slowly, and with reverence — because they can. The able-bodied pilgrims ask to wheel Jamie to the Grotto. They start out the week thinking they are taking him. By the end of the pilgrimage, they know that Jamie brought them to the Grotto.

The life I now live in the body, I live by faith in the Son of God, who loved me and gave himself for me. (Gal. 2:20, NIV)

Living Catechist Jamie

Jamie lives in a group home in Minneapolis. Jamie traveled to Rome, Italy, to meet with the Apostolica Penitenzieria, the oldest office of the Vatican, to appeal for a plenary indulgence for faithful through Our Lady of Lourdes Hospitality North Ameri­can Volunteers. Jamie continues to be sponsored to make a pilgrimage to Lourdes every year to refuel his tank with grace.

Q83443-Everyday_Miracles-Ebook-25

A

Chapter 8

Last Obstacle to Total Love

Wishful Andrea

Andrea came from a loving Italian-American New Jersey family so close to the Pennsylvania border that they jokingly would say they came from East East Philadelphia. Andrea was more than pretty; she was noticeably beautiful. Andrea was so attractive that she could have pursued a career on the runway or television. As smart as she was beautiful, Andrea chose science for her education and profession. Kind, intelligent, and creative, she was too sweet and wholesome for anyone to truly envy. Any young woman would want to be Andrea — until her leukemia diagnosis.

Cancer is too often a daunting, ugly, and protracted battle. Chemotherapy, failed bone marrow transplants, and platelet infusions robbed Andrea of the years after college when she should have been sunning carefree on the Jersey Shore with her girlfriends. But instead of leisurely wasting time, Andrea was wasting away. In between her marrow transplants, a close school friend of Andrea’s was ordained a Catholic priest. Fr. Chris strongly urged (that’s Italian for insisted) Andrea and her parents to make a pilgrimage to Lourdes, France. He told them the world-famous Catholic shrine is the most renowned holy place for healing and miracles — and Andrea needed both.

As faithful Catholics, her parents committed to trying to take their daughter to Lourdes. It would not be simple or easy. Medically, there were serious risks for Andrea to travel. Her immune system was compromised by ongoing extensive treatments. Rare platelets would need to be arranged with the small local Lourdes hospital tucked away within the Pyrénées Mountain range in the southwest of France. Courageous physicians and nurses signed on as volunteers, paid their own expenses, and dedicated their sacrificed vacation time to provide necessary delicate medical supportive care for Andrea to be able to join the pilgrimage. Her Jewish doctor supported Andrea and her parents, as if prescribing a spiritual medicine in caring for her soul as much as he prescribed extensive treatments for her body.

Andrea scientifically understood exposure risk, especially in confined spaces with crowds. Millions of pilgrims visited Lourdes each year. Although it would be a personal journey for Andrea, it would not be a private pilgrimage. Andrea would be exposed to numerous people with incalculable germs. Any travel could shorten her life by days or weeks. She no longer had months left to forfeit in a measured risk. Andrea was a scientist. She understood. But Andrea was not average or above average. Andrea was extraordinary. A young woman of faith, she was wise beyond her age. According to her prognosis, time was precious. Andrea knew it. She decided to go to Lourdes anyway.

Fr. Chris joined Andrea and her parents to travel together. At the gateway airport they met the medical team who had carefully planned her supportive care throughout international travel and stay abroad. Like insurance, hoped to never be needed, but smart to have, a plan was painstakingly prepared for death en route and in France. That was how critically ill Andrea was in the weeks and days prior to their planned departure. Lourdes Volunteers, Andrea, and her family were prepared, or as prepared as can be imagined in such a heavy-hearted situation.

Andrea was weak. Adamantly, she refused a wheelchair at JFK airport in New York. Passengers using wheelchairs pre-boarded the plane for Paris. Andrea distanced herself from the visibly “sick and disabled” group, insisting she stand in line with the seemingly healthy able-bodied passengers waiting to enter the aircraft. At first glance, this might have made sense. Passengers using wheelchairs could be sickly with germs that Andrea should avoid. It was a long wait. Even the supposedly healthy passengers were tired by the time the plane was fully loaded for departure.

Cancer and aggressive treatments tap into a different world of fatigue and exhaustion. It is as if the vibrant life-giving source within a soul has been sucked out, leaving behind only a wisp of the former self — like a flimsy wobbly copy of what was once a vigorous life. Halfway down the ramp, the team halted. Andrea had become too weak to take another step without some rest. Promptly, she was perched atop a carry-on suitcase and leaned against the jet bridge enclosure for a makeshift respite. Andrea did not protest or complain. She smiled sweetly. After a brief regrouping of her depleted strength, Andrea summoned herself and steeled her body, drawing from an empty reserve to somehow will herself to her seat. Although Andrea was fragile, she was still shiny through a deep inner grace.

Travel abroad sapped the last whisper of her remaining strength. Andrea required platelet infusions shortly after arriving in France. Totally depleted, she could no longer fight the wheelchair needed to transport her. The Lourdes Volunteers Medical Team doctor, nurse, and a Sanctuary priest accompanied the family to the small yet sophisticated local hospital. They were attentive to every preplanned detail. The sensitive transfusion went well.

Lacking the energy to put one foot in front of the other, Andrea had no choice but to be wheeled to the dining room and scheduled pilgrimage events. After her French hospital adventure, the only possible way out of her hospital bed in the Accueil Notre-Dame was to be seated in a wheelchair. Most people are hesitant to use a wheelchair for fear it will become a permanent situation instead of a temporary solution. It was a different fear for Andrea, who was hiding an embarrassing secret. Sweet Andrea skeeved people in wheelchairs. Skeeve (sometimes pronounced schkeeve) is an American-Italian Jersey slang expression of repulsion, a negative term describing an instant reaction of discomfort for a person, place, or thing.

Andrea never expressed her fear of people with disabilities or in wheelchairs. She was sincerely polite to everyone she encountered — walking or wheeling. People in wheelchairs just made her extremely uneasy. She didn’t know why or when this came about. Possibly, it was from nursing home visits as a child or a deep intuition that she would be counted among them someday. Why or when no longer mattered. Her discomfort always seemed to be with her. She wished it was not so. It was an instinctual response she could not stop, avoid, or control. Immediately beneath her disdain, Andrea had genuine tender compassion, but she did not know how to bypass or override her instantaneous skeeving reaction. St. Bernadette used to say that our first reaction belongs to us in our humanness, and that our second reaction belongs to God within us. As we grow closer to God, the time between our human and holy reactions is reduced and can disappear. Just arriving in Lourdes, Andrea had not yet learned this from Bernadette. Somehow, she had managed to conceal her secret skeeve throughout her life. Nobody knew. She was too embarrassed to admit it openly and too ashamed to discuss it with anyone. Andrea discreetly found a way to unnoticeably avoid people in wheelchairs — until Lourdes. That would prove utterly impossible in Lourdes.

The sick and disabled are the Very Important Persons, the “VIPs,” honored at Lourdes everywhere — and they are everywhere! There is a special place of privilege for the sick and disabled at every event and in every place within the Sanctuary, and up in the public town. Inevitably, Andrea would be wheeled and seated next to, between, or nearby someone else in a wheelchair at every place in Lourdes. She could no longer avoid her secret.

Andrea found herself eye-to-eye with others in their wheelchairs. Being at eye level changed everything. Intimate seated conversations revealed a profound understanding of interior connectivity and innate wisdom. The exchanges were simple, wholly humble, and holy inspired. The wheels, and possibly her dependency, smoothly stripped away the façades protectively covering her. Patience, humor, and gratitude were noticeably present among the wheelers — and notably less amid the fast-paced walkers distracted by their constant movement of busyness. Seated stillness afforded Andrea a heightened sensitivity of thoughtfulness that led to a deep insightful grace — like an infused biblical wisdom. Quickly, Andrea fell in love with her newfound wheeling friends, especially Dave, her newfound “Lourdes Brother” in her new pilgrimage family.

Individually and collectively, Andrea was fully embraced without hesitation and returned the love completely. It was “love without measure,” as Bernadette had said. Andrea was learning — not a mushy crush or pity, but rather a true love of seeing Jesus Christ seated in the wheelchairs across from her, next to her, in front, and behind her. It was a transcending love — holy, eternal, and total love. Andrea was de-skeeved through an unexpected life-changing grace she never knew she needed. This was not because she was in a wheelchair herself, but because she had overcome her “last obstacle to total love” through looking into the eyes of Jesus Christ.

Although Andrea was of Italian descent, she did not know the particular story of a radical man from Italy who changed the world, St. Francis of Assisi. In the thirteenth century, as a young man returning home broken after battle, Francis experienced an extraordinary conversion. Biblical lepers (now respectfully called Hansen’s Disease patients) were ostracized as untouchables (skeeved), as there was no cure for the dreaded Old Testament disease. At the onset of his conversion, Francis was growing in love, except for lepers. Like everyone in his time, he feared contagious leprosy. One day, Francis saw a sore leper on the road, alone and in need. As was customary, he quickly turned away. Suddenly, infused with a grace, he was moved with deep compassion; he stopped and dismounted his horse. Francis fully embraced the suffering leper with love. Turning around, Francis realized the leper had mysteriously disappeared. He understood that he had just embraced Jesus Christ. His heart was expanded, magnified in love, through and in Christ. Francis overcame his “last obstacle to total love!” Soon after his life-altering embrace of the leper, Francis “left the world” in a radical abandonment of the luxury and comfort afforded to him by his family. Francis decided to live in the world, but not of the world. He chose to live amongst lepers, outcasts, and the sick to selflessly care for them. He lived in simplicity, embracing poverty amidst a materialistically obsessed society. Francis detached himself from the secular world. Others soon joined him. His followers became the first Franciscans. Caring for the sick and those on the margins of life, Franciscans continue today to love the poor and the sick in the spirit of their seraphic father, Francis.

Andrea overcame her last and secretly hidden obstacle to total love. She loved her most feared untouchables, wheelchair-to-wheelchair. Andrea explained that her heart had exploded, blown wide open to magnify her capacity for love by loving everyone she met in their wheelchair while seated in hers. When she stood up on stronger days, the love expanded to everyone she encountered, standing or seated. Like St. Francis, Andrea understood that she had overcome her final fear to love without boundaries. She was transformed. The story of St. Francis resonated within Andrea. Although she had never heard it before, she now had a name and explanation for her holy experience. Andrea wanted to tell everyone about the value of a pilgrimage to Lourdes. It was the eternal healing she needed most, more than a physical cure.

After returning home, Andrea wrote a tender letter to her family and friends trying to explain the life-changing experience and great deepening of her faith she had received on her pilgrimage. Andrea was thankful and grateful. She knew her wish for anyone in need of healing or a grace to go to Lourdes was not financially possible for everyone. Some families could never afford the gift of international travel that her family was blessed to afford for her.

A talented amateur photographer, Andrea took her pilgrimage pictures and created artistic Lourdes note and prayer card sets. Painstakingly, she printed the images, hand-cut delicate scalloped edges, and then folded and wrapped them in silk ribbon. She selflessly gave of her limited hours to design and create her card sets. Like Andrea, the cards were beautiful. They were the note cards she wanted to find and buy in Lourdes, but were not available in the many shops surrounding the Sanctuary. She hoped others who might want them would give a small donation for a set of her cards to help send someone to Lourdes. It was a small yet practical beginning to spur a sponsorship fund where a formalized one did not yet exist with Lourdes Volunteers. It was about to become her gracious legacy.

Andrea died six weeks after returning from France. Her parents shared her passionate letter at her calling hours. In response to her letter, $26,000 in donations in honor of Andrea were received by Our Lady of Lourdes Hospitality North American Volunteers. The donations arrived in checks, small and large, most often with compassionate notes. There were no specific instructions received other than a few marked “Andrea’s Fund,” which did not exist, but was to be born out of love for her and her wish. The fledgling apostolate had never received such a large sum of money. Andrea’s Wish needed to be officially established by Lourdes Volunteers. As the Association was already a non-profit charity, attorneys and accountants legally accomplished what was required to formally establish the designated fund within the organization. Whatever was needed to be known to administer the fund was either written in her letter or lived in her pilgrimage experience. Anyone who needed to go to Lourdes should be able to make a pilgrimage — exactly her wish!

All the funds were used in the first year, in time for the 2008 Lourdes 150th Jubilee, when the record number of pilgrims swelled to almost ten million! The next year $63,000 was received and disbursed — more than twice the funds of the inaugural year. Doubling again the following year, Andrea’s Wish went on to sponsor more than $1,000,000 for special needs pilgrims and their family members in its first decade. There is never a surplus, only “our daily bread” needed to sustain in the moment. Andrea’s Wish might seem beyond dreams or logic, but continues through the generosity of many, mostly in small gifts, accumulating to sponsor one pilgrim at a time. Continuing to provide sponsorship to “anyone who needs to go to Lourdes” is a testament to Andrea’s total love and her holy wish.

Most High, glorious God, enlighten the darkness of my heart and give me true faith, certain hope, and perfect charity, sense and knowledge. (Prayer of St. Francis of Assisi)

Wishing Total Love

Andrea’s Wish sponsors pilgrims to Lourdes each year. Andrea’s parents continue to support Lourdes Volunteers and remain active within the apostolate. In early 2020, they traveled as valuable members of a plenary indulgence team representing different aspects of Lourdes Volunteers to the Vatican in Rome.
Q83443-Everyday_Miracles-Ebook-26

A

Chapter 9

A Liquid Grace Fills the Hole

Scottish Sheena

Sheena is a sweetly soft-spoken Scottish woman and mother of seven grown children. Although she met and married a United States Marine when she was nineteen, has lived in Georgia and Virginia, and recently retired as an American citizen with her husband in Pennsylvania, her lovely Scottish accent remains delightfully unmistakable whenever she speaks. Sheena was always quiet and reserved, never wanting to draw attention to herself. She would choose a seat in what is known in the American Bible Belt Deep South as the “amen corner” of any church she visited. This place would be the furthest back space for discreet worship where someone could go to be intentionally unnoticed in prayer. That’s why it was surprising when Sheena came forward as the first woman to officially sign up with Lourdes Volunteers to serve in France. A humble modern-day Bernadette joined the fledgling hospitality with a group of volunteers from her parish. In May of 2003, two groups arrived in Lourdes back-to-back for service as new volunteers at the Lourdes Sanctuary. The group bonded quickly as a family in the spirit of their new hospitality. They were helpful and encouraging to each other throughout the week and would continue to be so for years afterward, remaining bound together as family-friends.

The presence of native English speakers was needed at the Sanctuary in Lourdes. Over half of those who made the pilgrimage to France spoke English as a first or second language. Yet, less than ten percent of the volunteers were fluent English speakers. Language and interpretation had sometimes proven to be a challenge in the international shrine. In the Sanctuary priorities, speaking English for pilgrims began to push against the long-standing requirement to speak French to communicate with fellow volunteers. It was also exceptional to have volunteers arrive in Lourdes for the first time for service. This made for a more complex week than Europeans coming to dedicate service after years of experience with their annual diocesan pilgrimages or having made school trips or family holidays to Lourdes. Without this past foundation of knowledge to build upon, a more complete introduction to pilgrimage, the Sanctuary, and the different services was needed for the new American hospitality. Additionally, it was unusual to have entire groups of volunteers. Individuals or a few friends together would typically come in service, but not groups of sixteen to forty first-timers. The beginning years of these introductory weeks of service were difficult, both for those trying to manage the influx of volunteer groups and for the groups of typically monolingual, non-French speakers themselves.

Sheena was registered to serve in the Piscines (the baths). Piscinierès (female bath attendants) perform a demanding service of assisting women from around the world seeking the experience of being immersed in Lourdes Water from the Grotto. Some of the sick who arrive are profoundly disabled and enter inside on stretchers or in wheelchairs, while others are walking wounded. Healthy tourists also come, curious, or with deep longing, generally seeking or in search of something specific or unknown. Lourdes and the spring of water from the Grotto are world renowned for miraculous cures and conversions. When non-Catholics are asked why they visit the Catholic shrine, they often reply that they came for a cure or the peace to accept their situation, “because it works!” Sometimes, it takes a while for the liquid grace to sink in, but the grace of Lourdes eventually penetrates deep within to quench the parched recesses of even the weariest or driest of souls.

For an experience as physically intimate and deeply spiritual as the baths, the Mother of God certainly chose the most suitable country. The dignity of the experience is ensured by the genteel decorum of French culture, with gracious French ladies who are sensitive to the delicate discretion needed to assist women in an intimately personal experience. The prayerful ritual is as gracious as the Mother of God. Modesty is protected for each person. Undressing, bathing, and dressing are tenderly accomplished by volunteers in the spirit of humble St. Bernadette. Sheena was grateful to be registered in the St. Jean Baptiste (St. John the Baptist) Piscine Service. She was pleased to help women from around the world get into their spiritually physical baths, and as a European American, she proved to be kindly well suited to this meaningful service.

There is continuous bending to both the left and right sides of the tub within the baths. Piscinierès in their beginning service week trade between tub sides and assist the women to dress and undress. Learning the techniques, most often by watching the example of others or being instructed in a foreign language, makes for a challenging transmission of a century and a half of expertise to novice first-timers. Trying to remember so many nuances of the protocol to assist a woman in her once-in-a-lifetime holy Lourdes experience can be daunting at best and easily overwhelming. The Piscines are frequently crowded with women often waiting for hours to enter. Inside, they are careful not to rush each person, but the pace can still seem rather quick to a new learner. Lourdes Volunteers came to refer to the fifth day of service as “Crying Thursday,” because it often proved to be the emotional breaking point for many new volunteers. For some, it is their first time witnessing physical suffering or such intense pain up-close, or their first close encounter to raw mortality, holding a dying woman or child before, during, or after their bath. Hearts and emotions can easily overflow, and they often do.

Sheena exited the Piscines in tears following her first Thursday afternoon service. Having been given a bath herself, she asked to meet the pilgrimage leader privately for a cup of tea.

Occasionally, a Piscine volunteer surfaces from service soaked in Lourdes Water, as if they literally immersed themselves in the figurative grace of the service. It remains a mystery how piscinierès can plunge their hands, wrists, and arms into naturally chilled mountain spring water countless times throughout each shift of service without noticing the cold. It is considered a miracle of service! Although repeatedly submerging their arms in the frigid-feeling water for hours, somehow a volunteer is always surprised at just how cold the water is when they bathe as a pilgrim themselves.

Sheena tearfully shared her observation of this phenomenon of the Lourdes Water, but there was much more on her heart. Sheena came as a volunteer and did not think of herself as a pilgrim, yet everyone who goes to Lourdes, knowingly or unknowingly, is on a personal holy journey. There are many graces of healing that come through Lourdes, often unexpectedly delighting volunteers. Maybe God knows it is only as a volunteer that these generous souls will go to the Grotto to receive a grace for themselves.

Sheena poured out her story over a pot of tea. She was born and raised on the Scottish coast, to the far north of Edinburgh. Religion was not a part of her schooling or family upbringing. Her parents were hard-working hotel restaurant managers. As with any small business, every family member was busy with their own responsible duties. An oil boom from the North Sea brought an influx of tradesmen with comfortable paychecks from Aberdeen to farther up the northeast of the United Kingdom. This brought Sheena and her family much-needed new business. A twenty-two-year-old frequent patron occasionally offered to help out behind the bar, affording him more time with a much younger Sheena than was prudent. With her parents preoccupied with tending to a prospering business, the inappropriate friendship went unnoticed until Sheena unexpectedly found herself pregnant at fifteen. He left immediately. As it turned out later, he had abandoned another girl the next town over in the same predicament.

The 1967 Abortion Act legalized the termination of a pregnancy up to twenty-eight weeks in Scotland. Back then, early-stage abortion was touted as the removal of unwanted clumps of undeveloped tissue and cells. Abortion soon became a controversy between medical scientists and religious leaders. It was not fully exposed or understood by the general public or the average person sitting in the pews or on a medical examination table. Without church affiliation, abortion was not a problem for Sheena’s family — abortion was the solution.

As an underage minor child, Sheena was legally subject to parental decisions. Her mother made an adult decision for her that was intended to be in her best interest. An appointment was scheduled for an abortion. Although she was lacking any spiritual influence or the technology of a sonogram, Sheena somehow felt inexplicably maternally protective. Her baby was unexpected, but was her baby. Unfortunately, she had no say in whether to keep or deliver the baby. Sheena was never defiant and rarely gave any trouble to her parents, teachers, or anyone else, for that matter. But in her feelings about her unplanned pregnancy, she remained defensively strong against her parents.

Four days after her sixteenth birthday, at twelve weeks pregnant, Sheena was literally dragged from her house to the car, kicking and screaming. The struggle had become physical when Sheena realized she was being taken to the hospital for an abortion. Her mother was insistent and pulled Sheena out of the house by her hair. A fistful of Sheena’s soft curls fell onto the hard pavement. Although she physically resisted, her mother’s controlling forcefulness won out. Sheena was pushed into the car, and then into the hospital.

Sheena was chastised by the maternity ward staff for having been with an older guy. Somehow, the adult man received no such criticism for being with someone of such a young age or for abandoning her and the baby. Sheena was hastily discharged and sent to her grandmother for one week, hidden away, in shame. She felt sickly with a fever of 103 degrees, which thankfully disappeared as quickly as it came. From that dreaded day in the hospital, nothing filled the unspoken hole forcibly carved out of Sheena on that nineteenth of May of what was hoped to have been her sweet sixteenth year.

Three years later, in her first full year of marriage, Sheena miscarried twice and secretly blamed herself. She knew nothing true of God and thought a punitive omnipotent was surely punishing her for what had happened. Her first three pregnancies left Sheena childless. Years later, her last pregnancy would also end in a miscarriage at twelve weeks. The guilt of four lost lives, especially the first aborted baby, was overwhelming and left Sheena deeply saddened. Her math simply did not add up. Her first baby was cruelly taken from her, and she mistakenly thought the next two were sacrificed as a punishment. Between the heavy bookends of lost lives, seven healthy babies were born, proving that Sheena was meant to be a mother and giving her the joy of motherhood!

Before her fifth baby was to be born, Sheena’s mother-in-law made a deathbed wish for her son to return to his childhood Catholic Faith. To honor her husband and his mother, Sheena sought out a Catholic priest for counsel. Unexpectedly, she found what she had been unknowingly missing and seeking her entire life — faith and a loving, merciful God! Sheena and her five children entered the Catholic Church together. She understood all the sins of her life right up to the moment of her Baptism were wiped away. Yet, the kindly, helpful priest could not fill the hole left by her first baby being forcibly taken from her. Being a good mother did not fill the hole created by all her babies not being with her. A busy life did not distract her enough to fill the hole within her. Absolutely nothing filled the hole — nothing.

The unfillable void never decreased and continued for over twenty years — until Sheena was immersed in the spring water at Lourdes. Sheena had not come seeking anything for herself. She simply came to help others. At Lourdes, “miracles” often unfold and transpire in this way. Over the outpouring of tea and tears, her deeply hidden secret steeped in shame seeped out into the open. Sheena asked in awe, “How is it possible to fill this hole today? How did this happen? Why here? Why now?” “A liquid grace filled the hole,” she said in awe, answering her own question. In selflessly giving of herself, Sheena received more than she gave.

Sheena understood her mother fully but wrongfully believed she was both protecting and helping her. Having been raised in shame as an out-of-wedlock child herself, her mother did not want her daughter or grandchild to endure the same difficult upbringing that culminated in her misguided decision.

Sheena did go on to complete her schooling as a practical nurse, graduated, married, and had a wonderful family. They lived and traveled around the world as her husband advanced his impressive career, taking them to live in exciting places. Life was good — except for the hole. A gaping hole had remained within Sheena for more than twenty years. Through Lourdes, she was healed of guilt, grief, and loss. The hole was finally filled, making Sheena whole again.

Post-abortive women often suffer a painful and sometimes debilitating sense of loss long after a difficult decision, often made secretly and rushed. Women sometimes believe they are unwelcome or excommunicated by the Catholic Church, without recourse, banned forever or unworthy to receive Holy Communion. This is untrue! Women and men cannot physically undo what has been done, but they can spiritually right the wrong through the grace of the Sacrament of Reconciliation.

Sheena found her healing unexpectedly in the baths at Lourdes. She says she measures her life as “before Lourdes” and “after Lourdes.” Everything was different for Sheena when her devastating grief was washed away in the baths, the grievous void soothed with a healing balm of grace. Her pastor sent a letter to Lourdes Volunteers after Sheena returned home. He beautifully wrote, “A light dimmed by suffering went to Lourdes in generous service, and a brilliant light returned to illuminate our parish and everyone she encountered.” He was a humble priest of a modest parish. In thanksgiving, he sent a personal, significant donation to ensure the luminous mystery of a confidential miracle at Lourdes could be available to another woman.

In another grace, Sheena held no resentment. She and her mother had a beautiful, loving relationship. The long-ago wound was healed longer ago. Sheena lovingly cared for her mother, never speaking a harsh word of her or their past. Mother and daughter talked every day and visited together as much as possible. Neither living across the Atlantic from one another nor a painful past decision kept them apart. Sheena was able to be in Scotland with her mother as she was dying in her advanced years.

There are many women who come into the Piscines with the same wound as Sheena. They whisper their secret, confidentially weeping on their way into the water. They cry tears of joy on their way out, in awe of the goodness and mercy of God. Interior healing is not considered as a physical cure in the Medical Bureau. Forgiveness or interior peace cannot be measured or x-rayed to be scientifically proven. Yet, miracles of the heart, miracles of the soul, and miracles of interior healing are some of the sweetest miracles of Lourdes. These profound cures and healings through the grace of Lourdes are not officially recognized in this world, but are certainly known to some here — and many more in the other world.

I would now like to say a special word to women who have had an abortion. The Church is aware of the many factors which may have influenced your decision, and she does not doubt that in many cases it was a painful and even shattering decision. The wound in your heart may not yet have healed. . . . But do not give in to discouragement and do not lose hope. (Pope St. John Paul II)2

Secondary Schoolgirl

Sheena returned to serve at Lourdes, bringing and sending four of her seven children in service. Until her mother died, Sheena spoke to her daily by phone and returned to visit her in Scotland as often as possible. Sheena and her husband are busier than ever with another grandbaby happily on the way!

2 Pope St. John Paul II, encyclical letter Evangelium Vitae (The Gospel of Life) (March 25, 1995), no. 99.

Q83443-Everyday_Miracles-Ebook-27
Q83443-Everyday_Miracles-Ebook-28

A

Chapter 10

’Til Death Do Us Part

Dolores and Alejandro

Dolores was ninety-eight years and eight months old when she boarded the plane to France. Several things were notable upon meeting Dolores: her chic sensibility with her custom-tailored Chanel-style jacket; her silky smooth skin; her captivating smile with tasteful lipstick; her well-chosen jewelry; and the pure, sweet love of her gentle husband, so attentive to her. It was delightfully charming and obvious that Alejandro was still totally smitten with Dolores after all their decades of married life. Although they had lived in Miami for years, Dolores didn’t speak English, leaving her doting husband to translate and do all the talking for her.

Alejandro had been a merchant marine traveling the world for his professional career. Both he and his wife emigrated from Ecuador to the United States. They settled in Miami as their favored port to spend their retirement years and the rest of their lives together. As faithful Catholics, their lives were centered in prayer, saintly devotionals, and the Sacraments. Living in South Florida for the climate, culture, and language was also a blessing to them in their senior time together. The handsome couple never had children, which seemed to bring them closer together and to make them more devoted to one another. Their niece lived nearby, also in Florida. She protectively checked in on her favorite aunt and uncle in their elder years.

Slowly after her birthday, nearing a century of life, Dolores descended into a quiet internalized distance, a faraway place from her usual spontaneous self. Dolores somehow lost her zest — and her zeal for everyday life along with it. Alejandro was also in his nineties, but a few years younger than his wife. He decided a holy pilgrimage to Lourdes was the best remedy for his cherished sacramental life-partner. Their beloved niece helped them to arrange their pilgrimage with Lourdes Volunteers, including a plan for her to stop by the Marian Sanctuary for a brief visit with them while she would also be nearby in Europe.

They took surprisingly few medications for their ages and had fewer care needs than many others on the pilgrimage, even those much younger than the near century-old spouses. The couple planned to care for each other as they always had — daily at home and whenever they traveled. They lived independently in Florida and planned to do the same while in France. They said they would need only limited assistance from the Lourdes Volunteers Medical Supportive Care Team.

Over the ocean, Alejandro was concerned when Dolores refused to eat or drink anything. He worriedly hovered over her as she abruptly pushed away her airline food tray. She wouldn’t take even a sip of water — nothing — she was adamant she didn’t want anything. On the way to the restroom accompanied by Alejandro, Dolores fell to the floor of the aircraft just over an hour before landing in Paris. She went down with a loud thud.

The Lourdes Volunteers Medical Team was at her side in moments. The well-trained and competent Air France flight attendants were alert, attentive and kind. Dolores was completely unconscious. With a cold cloth placed on her head and wrists by the pilgrimage nurse, she quickly awoke, feeling groggy. The pilgrimage physician gave her water to sip through a straw. Dolores wasn’t pleased to be the center of a fuss and was obviously less than thrilled to be instructed to drink fluids before trying to stand up, but she reluctantly complied.

After she recovered, the flight captain decided Dolores needed to deplane in Paris with an ambulance transport. He prudently required she be examined by an airline physician before being allowed to board the smaller plane to the South of France or an international return flight home. Alejandro, the pilgrimage physician, and one of the pilgrimage leaders deplaned with Dolores. The rest of the pilgrimage group traveled on to the connecting flight terminal scheduled to depart in a few hours to Pau, nearby Lourdes.

An estimated ninety-two million travelers arrive and depart via the Paris Charles de Gaulle (CDG) Airport in an average year. Unknown to almost everyone bustling about in the busy airport above, there is a small but efficient urgent care facility discreetly built underneath one of the major terminals. Dolores was swiftly moved on a stretcher from the back of the plane to a hospital bed in the secluded airport emergency room. A young French doctor entered the examining room wearing a United States Navy lanyard with his official CDG Airport badge securely attached. He was surprised to meet Americans and a fellow sea-loving merchant marine. He explained that an American naval physician had gifted him the lanyard — a Lourdes Volunteers Medical Team doctor he met in Lourdes during a previous May International Military Pilgrimage. What a small world and what a grace of Lourdes, they all agreed, smiling — it was a Lourdes-incidence! The young doctor conveniently spoke Spanish, making direct communication with Dolores and Alejandro comfortable and easier. Alejandro revealed that Dolores had briefly been hospitalized a few weeks before this international departure. She was pronounced “as healthy as can be for an almost centenarian” and discharged. He added that Dolores had lost her appetite over the past few months and had not eaten much before departure and nothing during travel.

After having something to eat and drink, Dolores went through several medical tests. A normal electrocardiogram (EKG) report, blood pressure, and blood sugar results were efficiently and swiftly presented to the medical team for evaluation. The doctor was impressed with both his Lourdes Volunteers Medical Team encounters, respectful of their care of the severely wounded veterans he had seen on their May pilgrimages and the attentive physician present to the elderly couple. The French airport physician felt confident Dolores was fit to travel, accompanied by the competent American doctor and the seasoned leader familiar with travel to Lourdes. He kindly cautioned Dolores to drink more fluids. Amazingly, the newly fed and hydrated pilgrim and her escort team were transferred by speedy ambulance to the connecting flight just in time to board the plane with their original travel group.

As the day continued, the medical team was increasingly concerned. Dolores had again refused all food and fluids since leaving Paris. Several hours after her arrival in Lourdes, Dolores was confused to find herself in a hospital bed in the Accueil, in France. She feared she would be hospitalized in Europe and could be separated from Alejandro. Dolores was becoming uncharacteristically belligerent, which is typical of someone suffering the onset of dehydration. One of the Spanish-speaking pilgrimage priests was called to administer the Anointing of the Sick because of her illness and her age, but she rudely dismissed him out of her room. Alejandro insisted this was not her usual temperament, and he was distressed by her escalating refusals, explaining this was only recent behavior and not at all like his beautiful wife of so many years.

She became upset with her husband and blamed him for her situation. For every harsh complaint she had about him, he had a lovely compliment for her. Alejandro described Dolores as a talented seamstress with fashion-designer taste. She was cultured, gracious, kind, and smart. He was blessed to be her husband. The proof of his care and all that he said being true was evident in her manicured nails, elegant clothing, tasteful jewelry, and his loving, attentive care for her well-being. Their niece called to ask if her aunt and uncle had safely arrived. She said her uncle was a holy and prayerful man who took wonderful care of his equally kind and faithful wife. They were worried about Aunt Dolores and hoped the pilgrimage to the healing shrine of Lourdes would restore her appetite and good nature, as well as dispel her recent melancholy.

The medical team called her primary care family practitioner in Florida to relay the situation and information from the French physician. A second Spanish-speaking priest was called in to offer an anointing. Dolores threw him out — at the door — faster than he could enter her room. She was dehydrated. The doctors probed Alejandro, and he admitted that dehydration was the cause of her recent hospitalization. A third priest, not a Spanish speaker, entered the room enchantingly singing in Latin while graciously gesturing a large Sign of the Cross in the open air. Gently taking her arm toward him to kiss her delicate hand in a dignified, formal, gentlemanly greeting, Father softly made the Sign of the Cross again, this time tracing it on her delicate hand. Through Alejandro’s interpreting, she agreed to receive fluids and to receive the Sacrament of Reconciliation with Anointing of Holy Oils. She even joked that not only could Alejandro translate, but he could also make an accurate list of her sins! The Spanish-speaking priest came in to hear her confession.

Everything changed after the Anointing of the Sick — instantly! Dolores explained that she was afraid to outlive Alejandro. She could not bear the thought of living in this world without him. She was always certain over the years that she would die before him because she was a few years older. Nearing one hundred years, she then began to worry she might live longer than he did! Alejandro said he knew she had become distressed over the past few months about her age. Her fear had somehow begun to outweigh her lifelong faith and her trust in what God had in store for them both. She seemed to slowly give up on life, a little at a time. Then suddenly, she lost interest in her favorite devotions, lively dinner conversations, and the meals themselves — refusing to drink, eat, or pray. Alejandro believed Dolores was suffering both physically and spiritually. Hydrated and anointed, her shiny personality and faith were restored, proving Alejandro right. They prayed together as they had for years, holding hands intertwined with their shared rosary beads and their stack of favored holy cards with devotional sayings.

As the week continued with the Message of Lourdes, prayer in the Grotto, and a visit to the baths, Dolores was renewed in her trust for whatever God knew was best for her and her husband. Like a wilted flower soaking up the refreshing moisture of a soft rain, Dolores perked up and blossomed with the grace received from the Sacraments. Being in a holy place surrounded by prayerful young university volunteers and faithful experienced helpers rejuvenated Dolores. Her appetite returned along with her interest in life with Alejandro — in the present, without so much fear for the near or extended future. She knew God had a plan for her and she announced that she would once again trust in Him. Surely, what God planned for her was better than she could imagine for herself, she said.

The young adult students and volunteers loved caring for Dolores and Alejandro. All the other married couples on the pilgrimage were grateful for the holy example of an elderly loving husband and his elderly wife as models of what they could aspire to be in their later years. One of the nurses caring for them said she learned what authentic, selfless love was — both when Dolores was ill and when she had recovered — because God is truly the silent third partner in a sacramental holy marriage. Dolores and Alejandro returned home to Miami filled with faith, living in the confidence of God for however many days they would have together, happily-ever-after in this world on their way to the other world.

So I will bless you as long as I live; I will lift up my hands and call on your name. (Ps. 63:4)

Happily Married Forever

Dolores is the oldest pilgrim to have made pilgrimage to France with Our Lady of Lourdes Hospitality North American Volunteers. Dolores died before Alejandro. He remained faithfully by her side until the time came when they would both be in the happiness of the other world.
Q83443-Everyday_Miracles-Ebook-29
Q83443-Everyday_Miracles-Ebook-30

A

Chapter 11

Blessed Daughter and Mother

Awesome Alley

Pre-teens tend to be sulky and moody, or erratic at best. Alexandra was no exception. Turning tumultuous twelve, she was crossing the tender terrain to close in on the dreaded emotional gap year of early adolescence. Petulant, insolent, and downright angry, she stormed into the terrible teens with gusto. Known as Alley, she was born an all-in kind of gal and would directly tell you so, if she thought you needed to know — or not. Her Russian-royalty bone structure and name, along with her fiery disposition, were inherent donations from her biological father. Alley only saw him occasionally during the first four years of her life and did not reconnect with him again until she went away to university as an adult. Her delicate features and sensitive heart were gifts from her attractive mother. Although twelve is not a pretty age, the desirable traits from both parents made Alley a beautiful, bright, and intelligent girl, even when she was brooding.

Alley knew she was adopted. She was four years old at her mother’s wedding when mom-and-me changed into we-three. Alley’s dad, the stepfather who was to raise her, loved her from their first meeting. When he’d moved into the same apartment complex as the mother-daughter duo, Alley’s mom presented them as a package deal, which appealed to the smitten military officer. He was instantly charmed by his new neighbors who, delightfully made him a husband and a father all in one day. Both his brother and uncle were Catholic priests, so faith was important to him, although he was never overt with his personal spirituality. Soon after marriage, Alley’s mother converted, and as they had always done everything together, Alley entered the Church alongside her mom. Four babies soon followed one after another, propelling the happy trio into a hectic, Catholic-sized family of seven.

Throughout Alley’s upbringing, her mother increasingly struggled with severe depression, which affected her marriage and motherhood duties. She suffered sporadic bouts of melancholy and anxiety, which she likely would have experienced regardless of her family situation. Back then, postpartum depression was called the “baby blues” and considered a natural, self-correcting occurrence for a small percentage of new mothers. But as a woman with a history of depression before childbirth, she was left grappling with mounting feelings of intense anxiety and insecurity that she did not know how to escape as the size of her family and the demands of parenting grew. Her depression was likely exacerbated by the adjustment to the constant uprooting of a military spouse life when she was moved often and far away from her friends’ and family’s support.

Alley was a sensitive and sweet child who was easy to care for and love. Everyone was happy to be around her because she was such a lovely little girl. At seven years old, that all changed when a neighbor sexually abused her, robbing her innocence. She did not understand what was happening, though she knew it was wrong. Afterward, she was terrified that her parents would find out. As is typical with sexual abuse victims and children, Alley felt it was her fault. She convinced herself that she was responsible. She felt guilty, damaged, and unworthy of love.

The combination of shame wrapped in guilt is typical for victims of sexual abuse, especially children. Tragically, the extensive psychological and emotional damage caused by the trauma of a sexual violation reverberates deeply within a victim for years, often for decades, most often for an entire lifetime. Moreover, Alley was extremely worried about adding to her mother’s problems. Without emotional stability at home, she was unable to confide her devastating betrayal to her mother. Alley was afraid to do anything that would disappoint her mom, make her angry, or add to her anxiety and depression. Alley said nothing. Ashamed, Alley held the abuse she suffered as a dark secret: a hefty burden for a seven-year-old child.

As time went on, she did not tell anyone about her suffering, instead stuffing it down where it festered for more than a decade. Throughout this time, her mother’s depressive episodes worsened in both intensity and frequency, making her behavior increasingly erratic and unpredictable. As a survival mechanism, Alley instinctively grew a thick protective skin, making herself resilient. But, deep down inside, this caused anger and resentment. Alley felt that no one understood her. She had to be strong all the time to hold in her pain and her burdensome, festering, dirty secret. Nowhere and nobody was safe or trustworthy.

Family life became more complicated for Alley and her parents. Being the oldest, Alley often took the brunt of the rage and depressive episodes her mother suffered. She felt protective of her younger siblings, trying to shield them from seeing their mother’s self-destructive behavior when her illness spun out of control. Alley felt alone as she entered into the tumultuous twelfth year of her hidden and tortured young life. She was in pain, hating herself for what had happened. She was crying inside for someone to notice, for anyone to understand. Suddenly, Alley was no longer thrilled to be adopted or Catholic. Sensing turbulence ahead, her Air Force dad was as prepared as possible for a first-time father of a pre-teen daughter embracing her strong-willed Russian temperament. Throw in the complications of secret sexual abuse, adoption, and her mother’s struggles, and it was undeniably apparent a rough ride was ahead or would have been — except for the unexpected grace of Lourdes.

Moving around the world became their routine; it was the typical military family way of life. They eventually wound up in the exotic Azores, where they met the holy couple who were to become Alley’s Godparents as she entered the Catholic Church. Years later, both families were thrilled to be stationed together again, this time in Germany. The Catholic youth leader there was planning a unique opportunity for teens to make a mission trip to Lourdes, France. It was a stretch to ask families to send a teenager such a far distance in a new program. Alley’s Godmother wanted her only daughter to join, but her juvenile diabetes proved a challenge, as most overnight camps or programs refused to accept insulin-dependent teens. Working through the practicalities, it was decided she would be welcomed to Lourdes, affirming the precedent for the new hospitality to do all possible to bring everyone to the Grotto — including volunteers, especially youth.

Alley’s Godmother offered to be an adult chaperone with her diabetic daughter and, soon after, requested her younger son also join them. He was only eleven years old. Surmising a one-week break as a needed reprieve for her good friends and their oldest, she invited her twelve-year-old Goddaughter to join them. The minimum age to join the mission service pilgrimage trip was fourteen. At twelve and eleven, they were too young. The youth leader called Lourdes Volunteers and appealed for the underaged youth to be allowed to join the group. Pleading, she said she just knew they should come to Lourdes. “Sometimes, you just know,” she begged. It was impossible to refuse her passionate plea.
