Chapter 16

Revenge Reversal

Southern Cynthia

Slouched down and hunched over in an airport wheelchair, Cynthia failed in her distressed attempt to go unnoticed. She was barely hidden behind a pillar in the bustling Baltimore-Washington International Airport. An observant passerby might have assumed she was desperate to disappear. Cynthia was dismayed at her visibility in the brightly lit baggage claim area of a crowded major airport. She could not make eye contact with anyone. She was polite and soft-spoken, and it was evident she was not belligerent or hostile, just too out of place for small talk, or more accurately, any talk at all. The medical team was prepared, having surmounted consistent obstacles to make her international travel possible. Comfort was a far stretch beyond Cynthia and had been for a very long time. She threw off any thoughts of comfort on her way out the door to try to get anywhere, one pain-filled struggle at a time. Going to Lourdes was frightening for Cynthia, but she was going, despite herself and her crippling fear.

Cynthia had a sweet southern drawl, the thick kind of accent ingrained with deep roots planted by generations far back in her Alabama family tree. Her granddaddy graduated from Harvard and brought his charming drawl safely back down south along with his prestigious medical degree. Even in her worst distress, Cynthia remained a lovely lady. Yet, she was obviously a deeply wounded woman, the type who flinches at every noise or jerks tautly in a startled response to every action. That was completely understandable considering what she had endured to survive.

The pilgrimage started out like any other. Several of the group required wheelchair assistance, including Cynthia. She was noticeable at excursion headcounts because her head was literally always down for the count. Every day held a different blessing in the thoughtfully planned schedule. Everything seemed wonderful until the “winter vomiting bug” invaded just before the departure back to the United States. In the fall of 2008, Cynthia and the others had embarked unknowingly on what would become infamously known to Lourdes Volunteers as “The Puke Pilgrimage.” A norovirus steamrolled across Europe and menaced its way into France, nearing the end of the Jubilee pilgrimage season. A gastrointestinal disaster descended upon Lourdes for several days.

Despite extra sanitary precautions, some volunteers and pilgrims came down with the virus and return travel mayhem ensued. Rushing through the lobby, the pilgrimage leader was greeted with a joyful hello, a surprise amid such stomach woes. Slowing down, she smiled in reply to the attractive lady seated across the way. She looked familiar, but she could not recall how they knew each other. Several minutes later, as she ran through the lobby again, the same woman called out to her, this time by her name. They definitely knew each other. The woman was attractive with classic style, sophisticated but not stuffy.

“Cynthia!” she exclaimed. Shocked, she added, “Cynthia! I didn’t recognize you! HOLY WOW! You look like a million bucks! You look like you just won the lottery!”

Cynthia replied, “Yes ma’am! I did! I won the spiritual lottery yesterday afternoon in the baths!”

These two women had just spent the past week together every day, yet one did not recognize the other! A life-changing impact was that visible in Cynthia’s appearance. She radically and literally looked like a new woman. Cynthia had been remade, refreshed, and renewed through a liquid grace.

Getting any special needs pilgrimage group back from France to the United States requires detailed planning. Lourdes Volunteers learned over time that after spending a week together in the Grotto, individuals bond along the way as a family in the charism of hospitality, which makes return travel easier, despite inevitable delays or misconnections. Even amid a nasty stomach upset, there was joy. Several who contracted the norovirus were unable to travel home. They were forced to stay in Lourdes with a small medical team, volunteer caregivers, and experienced leaders. Spending a few extra days in Lourdes did not seem like a burden to them, despite the unpleasant reason for the surprise bonus time in France. Nobody ever really wants to leave Lourdes. For those departing as scheduled, some adjustments had to be made to adapt and reconfigure manifests.

The New York airport overnight rooming was reorganized. Cynthia said she no longer needed a nurse, and the medical team agreed. The non-medical pilgrimage leader, who initially did not recognize her earlier that very same morning in a lobby in Lourdes, was now reassigned as her roommate that night. This was the first time the two ladies had an opportunity to spend private or uninterrupted time together. Although the leader had heard about Cynthia from the pilgrimage manager, Cynthia started her story from the beginning.

As a child, she was schooled by nuns who told her the stories of Lourdes and Bernadette. She and her groom both came from established and respected southern families. She married young, knowing nothing about alcoholism, substance abuse, or PTSD in Vietnam veterans. She had two beautiful babies in a few short years and it quickly became apparent that Cynthia needed to provide for them. Her husband was unable to work consistently. As his addictions spiraled out of control, so did his rage — in tandem with escalating spousal abuse. She desperately tried to stay married. Divorce was unknown in her family. “If you made yourself a hard bed, you had to sleep on it,” she said. It was just the way it was for generations where she came from.

While working, Cynthia put herself through school, earned an advanced degree in microbiology, and began her doctoral studies. Desperate to shield her young daughters from the trauma of their daddy’s ongoing violent outbursts, she reluctantly accepted divorce as their safest future. To protect her and her girls living alone, she did what any good southern gal would do, saying, “I got myself a dog and a gun.”

One night, the dog didn’t come in. Cynthia opened the door to call for their protector pet, shocked to be confronted by her ex-husband lunging up onto the porch from his hiding place in the bushes. Brutally, he beat her. He threw her around like a ragdoll, with fierce force. Dragged into the bedroom, after a particularly heavy blow, she fell limp onto the bed. “This is going from bad to worse” was her last conscious thought. It was worse than the worst she could have imagined. Cynthia did not realize her loaded shotgun intended to protect her had slipped out from under her bed and into his grasp to be used against her. He shoved the double barrels into her gut and pulled the trigger. He ran, leaving her for dead.

Cynthia fell from the bed onto the floor in a large pool of her own blood. She had no idea how she dragged herself to the phone or how she was able to call for help, other than she was begging God to let her live long enough to raise her little girls. She was pleading with a mother’s love for her daughters. God heard her, she said. Against all medical odds, Cynthia survived. The surgeon told her the reason why all those cowboys in the old western movies grab their bellies and bleed out to a dramatic on-screen death is because most gunshot wounds to the gut do just that — they kill you dead.

Cynthia spent the better part of a year hospitalized. Her ex-husband was convicted and sentenced for attempted manslaughter instead of attempted murder. He didn’t bring the loaded gun with him, he just happened upon it. Therefore, the courts deemed it was not pre-meditated, she explained. Cynthia said she knew he had come to kill her. He had threatened to kill her many times before and had beaten her many more. She and many others thought that his conviction and sentencing were far too lenient.

Thirty-three surgeries over eleven years did not close the open wound in her abdomen, including three failed extensive skin graft attempts. Constant infections plagued any possibility of healing the tissue or closing the open hole. A gregarious, fun-loving lady was shot down to a worrisome woman, a withered introverted version of her former self. Cynthia lived smaller. She described her life as being drastically reduced since the violent shooting. The impact on her as a victim of brutal domestic violence cascaded onto her daughters. The girls lost their successful, independent, and resilient mother they had always known. Cynthia was a debilitated and dysfunctional shell of her former self.

Cynthia was increasingly afraid of the impending release of her ex-husband from prison. In her opinion, he was rightfully convicted of shooting her, but in her pain and practical reality, she felt he was wrongfully given too short a prison sentence for trying to kill her. Cynthia knew that her suffering would unfairly continue long after he had served his unjust sentence to soon be a free man again. Cynthia also had fearful concerns about her daughters being around their father after his release. She was always worried or scared, most often both. Her mounting fear became terror, and her growing resentment turned into seething hatred. Cynthia simply needed her ex-husband gone. She confided that she had promised God if she “did not have to scoop out that pus from this wound every day, she would not have to get rid of that evil man.”

Often bed-bound, Cynthia happened upon a nun on television in her Alabama birth state. Just outside Birmingham, Mother Angelica founded EWTN, the largest Catholic Network in the world. This particular show was hosted by a priest and featured pilgrimages to Lourdes for the sick and suffering. Exasperated by the continuing failed surgical attempts to close her wound and her increasing concern over the impending release of her ex-husband from prison, Cynthia needed a miracle. She faintly yet fondly recalled the nuns from her childhood telling her about the miraculous Lourdes legends of healings and miracles.

Cynthia contacted Lourdes Volunteers, never expecting to go, but more out of a desire for healing from some of “that there miracle water” from the spring in France. The Head Nurse called Cynthia several times; they spoke at length and in detail in continuing conversations. Cynthia had damaged muscles that compromised abdominal support and reduced blood flow to her lower body. Walking more than short distances was no longer possible. She had limited stamina and seldom ventured out in public. A wheelchair would be needed for her to travel and during her stay in Lourdes. Worse, she dreaded the thought of being in public — and in a wheelchair. Worst of all, she was frightened of being away from home for a whole week. Cynthia suffered terribly and feared her daughters were suffering more. She persisted in her thoughts against travel and refused to go. It was simply impossible for her to make a pilgrimage. She settled on receiving Lourdes Water mailed to the self-imposed, safe cloister of her private home.

The Lourdes Volunteers nurse arranged for the office to mail Cynthia a bottle of Lourdes Water. Cynthia explained that, as a microbiologist, she knew pouring unsterilized liquid into an open wound highly susceptible to infection was not just unadvisable or unacceptable — it was unthinkable! Cynthia was a woman of science, but she was also raised in faith. She lay in bed with the little bottle in her hand. The wound opening was about the size of a small egg and regularly filled with an unending oozing substance that she was forced to clean out every day. She said a prayer, made the Sign of the Cross, and dumped the contents of the little bottle into the gaping hole. If she woke up in the morning with a massive infection yet again, God’s Will be done. She would return to the hospital for yet another round of extended intravenous treatments.

Cynthia awoke in the morning — stunned ! The wound had healed closed! After eleven long years and thirty-three unsuccessful surgeries, the gunshot hole in her abdomen had inexplicably closed and completely grown over while she slept! She no longer needed to scoop out the drainage or pus every day. Cynthia had made a desperate deal with God — and He kept His end of the bargain! Only one other person knew about her deadly plan for her convicted shooter, but they did not know about her deal with God. Cynthia was too deeply entrenched in vengeance to let go. She decided to cling to her vendetta, despite her miraculous wound healing with Lourdes Water. Her vicious hatred was greater than the hopeful promise she had made to God. Besides, only God and that one person knew. But God knew.

“Then that Lourdes Volunteers nurse kept calling me, insistin’ that I had to go.” Cynthia continued, “Good ol’ fashioned Catholic schoolgirl guilt put upon me by that dang redheaded nurse guilted me into going on that pilgrimage.” Ultimately, because the physical wound had healed, she felt obligated to go with the people who gave her the Lourdes Water for the healing. She reluctantly acquiesced. It was only after returning from the pilgrimage that she more fully understood why she really needed to go to holy Lourdes. The true reason was something only one other person and God knew, she whispered, secretively.

The bath experience in Lourdes involves both a spiritual and physical ritual. Unclothing can be a symbolic stripping of ourselves, of who we are, alone before God. Although the actual process is modest and bathers are not exposed before others, most come to intrinsically understand a vulnerable reality. Entering into the water with an awareness of interior imperfections can allow a submerged cleansing in a supernatural grace. In the baths, Cynthia’s hidden revenge was washed out, replacing the darkness within her with the Light of Christ. Her appearance was so shiny and her countenance so transformed that Cynthia emerged almost unrecognizable!

Cynthia was wholly sorry and genuinely repentant. She deeply regretted her intended plan against her former husband. She left the baths to pray in the Grotto on her way to the Chapel of Reconciliation. Through the ministry and help of a priest and the generosity of the Sacrament of Mercy, she reconciled herself to God. As she heard the words of Christ through the priest, “Your sins are forgiven. Go in peace,” she knew her sinful intent had been wiped clean during her contrite confession. Cynthia returned home transformed and peaceful. She dismissed her hateful plan against her ex-husband.

As Cynthia confided her life story to her pilgrimage leader, she lifted her pajama top to show the discolored indentation in her abdomen. New purplish-pinkish skin seemingly stretched across to cover the awkwardly deep indent. Her discolored skin was fresh looking, like newborn baby skin. Cynthia continued, explaining how she came to Lourdes, right up to walking out of the baths a new woman, surprised to be healed in an unexpected spiritual grace. The two ladies talked late into the night, like schoolgirls at a pajama party. They joked that they could sleep on their connecting flights home the next day, which they did.

Cynthia returned a changed woman, free from all bitterness and resentment. A few years later, she met and married a wonderful man. She learned to trust again. With the hatred gone, there was room for love and good things in life. Cynthia came to think of her former husband as the father of her daughters and not just the man who tried to kill her. She found joy again. Her daughters were grown and were becoming accomplished women. Her life was better than she could have possibly ever expected or dreamed possible during her darkest days of pain.

After her family and home were settled, Cynthia wanted to give back in some way for what she received. She returned to Lourdes as a volunteer in July 2011. The rooming situation was cramped. The same leader from her first pilgrimage was unexpectedly, again, assigned as her roommate, this time for ten full days. It was a joyful reunion. As before, they stayed up too late talking and laughing. So much so that the volunteers in the next room pounded on the thin walls, signaling that nightly outbursts of belly laughter were too loud for their neighbors attempting to sleep! They adjusted their voices to giggling and whispering late into the pleasant summer nights like treasured sisterly sessions.

Raised as a southern belle, Cynthia was gracious and oozed genteel hospitality. It was the traditional way of life across the south of the United States. She was organized, forward-thinking, quick-witted, and passionate about Lourdes. Her lovely way, charming southern accent, sense of humor, and leadership skills made her perfect to be a pilgrimage leader for Lourdes Volunteers. Cynthia agreed to lead groups. A three-year training would require her to return to learn in tandem with an experienced leader. Cynthia committed to helping bring others to the same grace that saved her from a tragic downfall, rebuilding her up to the happy and grace-filled life she was living.

During one of their intimate roommate chats, Cynthia showed her scar again. It was no longer deeply indented as it initially was right after the holy healing. The coloration lessened, but the scar remained as a visible call to prayer for Cynthia. She said her body held a constant reminder for her to pray for the father of her daughters.

At the conclusion of the service pilgrimage in Lourdes, Cynthia made a Declaration of Cure at the Medical Bureau. She said the Medical Director listened intently and took notes as he asked specific questions. He found her description of her open wound that was incurable for over a decade, having been spontaneously cured with Lourdes Water, to be worthy of consideration. An official dossier was opened. Cynthia said the medical director requested that she write down what happened to her and invited her to return the following year with her complete medical history to begin the investigation of the process of a scientific scrutiny. She said he told her the healing of her soul in forgiveness seemed a miraculously blessed gift to her that he considered to be truly profound, possibly more than a physical cure.

Five weeks after returning home from her volunteer pilgrimage, Cynthia died in a car accident. Reconciled to God through the Sacraments, Cynthia was prepared to meet God in a grace from Lourdes. The healing of her soul was then and forever surely more significant than her open wound closing overnight after eleven years. Cynthia was cured, blessed, and healed — physically, mentally, and spiritually — and prepared to meet God through a gift of the Grotto.

All bitterness, fury, anger, shouting, and reviling must be removed from you, along with all malice. Be kind to one another, compassionate, forgiving one another as God has forgiven you in Christ. (Eph. 4:31–32, NABRE)

Sweet Southerner

Cynthia was a devoted mother, a dedicated medical and science professional, a talented pianist, a southern cook, a trustworthy friend, and a passionate volunteer. She had a gracious flare, an ingenious wit, and a generous laugh — and all with a twinkle in her eyes! Cynthia was blessed to be living her life grace-filled and fully in the Sacraments after her return from Lourdes.

Chapter 17

Broken to Blessed

Bereaved Brian

Bereavement knows no boundaries. Grief is not an equal opportunity suffering. The death of a son or daughter is said to be the worst of losses. If mourning a child were a planet, it would be its own private solar system. It is a universe of hurt unto itself that is completely incomparable to other losses. We don’t think about it, but somewhere deep within us we expect to grow old and someday bury our parents. We might have to bury a sibling, or they will bury us — but it is against the natural order of life and the human heart to bury our children. It just is. Grieving such a debilitating loss is an unbearable tragedy that often breaks a parent, marriage, and families. Grieving a child never ends. Mom or Dad will never “get over it.” They will, hopefully, find ways to better manage their response to living without their son or daughter in this world. But they will never stop longing. The heart-wrenching grief will truly end only when the parent dies. Faith provides a small consolation because we know that our greatest family reunion awaits us in Heaven.

The first small group of wounded veterans and warriors was coming together with Lourdes Volunteers to join the annual Pèlerinage Militaire International (PMI), the International Military Pilgrimage at Lourdes. It was a unique group, and it quickly became apparent that a military priest and a military medical practitioner would be best suited to assist the military pilgrims and families. Wounded warriors and veterans had different needs than civilians; their comfort seemed to be in the trusted company of one another. All Lourdes Volunteers Special Needs Pilgrimages have a volunteer medical team and one priest chaplain for every twenty-five pilgrims. Never before had there been the added requirement to be a military service member or veteran to volunteer. The priest had written a request to come to Lourdes, but a military medical professional might prove difficult to find.

Brian’s name and number were given with a compassionate plea. His only son had died in a car accident the year before. Brian deployed to Afghanistan one month later, returning home a year later, having left the Church. Quite honestly, he was “pissed off” at God, he said in military style. He was a devastated dad, further bruised and broken by the damage of a long deployment into a consistently and heavily hit hot spot of conflict with intense medical demands. Military medical practitioners see the worst of what we can do to each other. Anyone in a medical command (officer or enlisted) can only save or fix so much. That’s a lot of compounded death and loss for any one soul to witness — and to remember and live with every day thereafter. Everybody knew Brian was walking, but wounded. Although he was hurting, he was able to function. He was fulfilling medical command duties and obligations to his private family practice at home. But he was vacant inside. His angry grief was fueled in the war zone, which was a frenzied feeding ground for a soldier with an endless supply of personal painful angst.

When Lourdes Volunteers contacted Brian, the conversation was curt and short. He was a busy officer who was accustomed to being in charge. The appeal sounded like some stupid civilian was wasting his time. Explaining the types of wounds some of the military pilgrims had, Brian listened, but only because there were service members involved. He was told if he did not come as the Medical Director, they probably could not go to Lourdes for the healing they hoped for. That grabbed his attention, along with something his Veterans Administration counselor had said a few days earlier: “If you lose your faith, you are lost.” She had recommended a pilgrimage, and now this woman on the phone was calling to ask him to join a pilgrimage — a rare word not often spoken or heard every day. As Brian had learned on the battlefield and in medical practice, there are few coincidences. His commander signed off in case Brian needed to help any wounded coming to France from Landstuhl, the U.S. military hospital in Germany where American wounded were medevacked from Iraq and Afghanistan. In the end, he did not need to do that, but his medical mission permission remained granted.

Brian agreed to be the Medical Director for the first Our Lady of Lourdes Hospitality North American Volunteers Wounded Warriors, Veterans, Service Members, and Military Families Pilgrimage. It started as a small group. Brian thought it sounded like there were more words in the pilgrimage title than there were seriously wounded pilgrims. The number of pilgrims grew quickly. His plan was to tell everybody to take two aspirins and call their primary care provider at their local Veterans Administration facility when they returned home. His thinking was that Lourdes is not a war zone and he wouldn’t have any real medical emergencies to handle. This one-week pilgrimage would be a piece of cake wrapped up as a sweet, free trip to Europe. Besides, he was required to do an annual out-of-country military medical mission. This would qualify. “Check that off the list,” he thought. It was not until the pilgrimage was over that he would reveal the real reason he agreed to come: to sip good Belgian beer in France at an outdoor café. He Googled Lourdes while the woman was pitching him on the phone and saw that trout were plentiful in the river running through the holy Sanctuary. He decided then that he would go to Lourdes and bring his fishing pole in his luggage, which he did. It was packed into his elongated duffel bag. Brian had no idea he was invited because those who knew him realized that he also needed to go to Lourdes for himself. It frequently happens this way. A person comes as a volunteer to help others (or to drink beer in bistros) and is blindsided by an unexpected grace in the form of conversion or inner healing.

Leisurely traveling to Lourdes on a Special Needs Pilgrimage is an oxymoron. Bringing disabled, sick, and dying people to France is arduous, difficult, and usually quite complicated. Brian seemed unapproachable. He was a gruff, stern, dismissive, and monosyllabic officer. He refused to wear a white lab coat. Lourdes Volunteers had figured out the fastest way to bring a group of volunteers together as a team was to easily identify who was who, clearly and up front — leaders in navy blue suits, medical professionals in white, and priests in collars. That made it easier for the leadership team to spot a needed doc-on-the-clock or nurse-on-the-spot or a priest from across a crowded airport or packed plane. Most volunteers see each other for the first time at the airport, never meeting one another before departure. It was also easier for pilgrims who needed to know who was who to ask for help with drainage bags, ex-fixators, pain medications, or oxygen levels. An additional, unexpected benefit was that airline personnel were able to easily identify who was responsible for the special needs passengers aboard their flight. It was a visual, color-coded, simple system that worked effectively. There was just one problem. Nobody wanted to wear white lab coats, nurse dresses, or scrubs in public or on an airplane.

Brian had completely disregarded everything the civilian know-nothing lady had said on the phone anyway. He didn’t bring his lab coat. Then, while the group was struggling to get through the airport security with a turtle shell brace for a broken back, medical devices, bags of medications, wheelchairs, and more, Brian realized not a single person in the group was looking to him for direction. He was an Army Major. He knew how to be in command, but nobody was listening to him because they could not see that he was the pilgrimage Medical Director. His rank was invisible because he was not wearing his white lab coat “uniform” or his military uniform. He was dressed like the pilgrims, in casual clothes, and he didn’t realize he was brooding. He looked exactly like the other suffering veterans — because he was one.

Brian quickly understood that the white coat would have helped; the color-coded dress system really was effective, similar to the military. While changing planes in Paris, the leader asked him if he had brought his lab coat with him. “No,” he smirked, adding it was too bad she wasn’t fully prepared with a size forty-four for him to throw on. Much to his surprise, she reached into her briefcase and pulled out his exact-sized lab coat, brand new, still in its plastic covering. She knew he would need it, and she would need him to wear it. Who just happens to carry the right size lab coat with them? It wasn’t until a few years later that he figured out that it was not by happenstance. She had purchased a lab coat for him, anticipating he would not bring his from home. There are few coincidences when it comes to Lourdes. Everything is either well planned or totally out of grace — or both. The experienced pilgrimage leaders attest to it, saying Lourdes is grace-upon-grace.

Getting off the last plane, the leader noticed blood running out of one of the sailor’s ears and drying down his neck. She asked if he had a problem hearing during the flight. He immediately but kindly dismissed her. It was just a ruptured ear drum; it was nothing compared to the soldier with the broken back in front of him. Brian was discreetly summoned to observe the situation and take care of him. He did. Listening to the deep cough of another veteran, Brian was again summoned. It turned out to be untreated bronchitis, flying into pneumonia upon landing in Pau, the last stop on the way to Lourdes. With a local Veterans Administration (VA) medical facility and military benefits accessible to him back home, the question was raised why he had not been prescribed an antibiotic before flying out. He replied, “Because, ma’am, nobody was shooting at me.” He didn’t think a serious chest cold was worth bothering his local VA primary care provider.

“Busy Brian” had no time for trout fishing or beer drinking. The hidden wounds of war quickly surfaced to be seen as the most serious needs. It seemed that sometimes the body stubbornly did not heal outside when harboring brokenness inside. Each person on the pilgrimage was suffering within. Their supportive care physical needs were swiftly reduced to secondary issues. The real hurting was the wound of the soul, and Brian was one with them.

On the first full day of the pilgrimage, Brian waited in line at the Grotto, doing whatever spiritual things were scheduled or expected of him and others to “get ‘er done while in Lourdes.”

Brian was surprised to hear the sweet voice of the Blessed Mother in the Grotto. She told him, “I know what it means to lose your only son. I am caring for your son here, like I cared for mine there.” Brian thought of the Pietà — the famous Michelangelo sculpture of the Blessed Mother holding her Son taken down from the Holy Cross. Standing in the Grotto, Brian knew Our Lady was holding his son — and that she understood. Brian was changed right then and there. All of the anger drained out of him, making room for holy consolation and healing. His only son, his fishing and hunting buddy, his best friend, was not coming back to this world. He knew that. But now Brian was consoled in the comfort that the Mother of God was holding him until they would be united together again in the happiness of the other world.

Brian was still bereaved, but his grieving was transformed into holy grieving. He came back to his lifelong Catholic Faith. The Sacraments of Reconciliation and the Holy Eucharist fortified a suffering father to endure grieving for his son and the traumatic impact of his war-zone deployment so soon afterward.

The pilgrimage was an astounding combination of veterans who understood one another and had come together in a holy place of healing. They somehow became the needed strength to help each other accept grace and healing. The priest was an active-duty colonel who was only days out of the field, with sand still in the creases of his uniform fresh from his Operation Iraqi Freedom deployment. He truly knew what they were suffering. Father was on duty on behalf of the Divine Physician with the Sacraments. Many of the wounded veterans had been away from the Church since deployments. Many were deployed multiple times, and for extended periods. After consciously disconnecting to be able to separate in the first place, reconnecting back home with family created stressed marriages. The chaplain was as busy as the medical practitioner, and they worked well together, in synchronized tandem. Brian tended to medical supportive care needs and listened to interior wounds, then made referrals to Father for spiritual needs. The priest did the same, but the other way around. One at a time, each veteran found interior healing and peace — every single one. It was absolutely amazing — miraculous graces!

Boarding the plane back home, Brian promised he would return the following year to help more veterans have a holy experience of healing in the Grotto. Once in Paris, Brian called the pilgrimage director who had stayed back in Lourdes with the volunteers to clean up after the pilgrims. His voice was so pleasant and the conversation so friendly that she did not immediately realize it was “Brisk Brian” calling. When she asked who it was, he replied, “Brian!” She asked, “Brian who?” His voice and temperament were completely unrecognizable! That is how profound a change had taken place in Brian in just one week in Lourdes. When he returned to his medical command, his sergeant, the medic he deployed with to Afghanistan, suggested the command send more wounded warriors to France. As a Muslim, he had never heard of Lourdes and did not know the lady there. All he knew was that Brian was broken, they couldn’t fix him, and that Lady in Lourdes sent him back from France fixed. The Protestant army general did not know about Lourdes or the Lady either, but seeing Brian restored, he encouraged the wounded to make the journey the following year. The Lourdes Volunteers Special Needs Military Pilgrimage became known as the “proven-effective program” to offset suicide and for healing of the stubborn interior wounds of war.

Brian made a commitment to return and to invite more military medical volunteers to help. In doing so, he made going on pilgrimage possible for hundreds of wounded warriors, veterans, service members, and military families. Brian especially took veterans suffering with Post-traumatic Stress Disorder (PTSD) to the Grotto — night or day — to find peace and healing, just as he had found. His faith restored at Lourdes, Brian helped many of his brother and sister veterans find faith and healing in the Grotto, under the loving gaze of the Mother of God.

I do not promise you the happiness of this world, but of the other world. (The Immaculate Conception in the Grotto)

Without Medical Coat

Brian keeps his promise to be a Medical Director for the Special Needs Pilgrimage and continues to make it possible for anyone who needs healing on a pilgrimage to go to Lourdes with supportive medical care available throughout travel and stay in the Sanctuary.
Q83443-Everyday_Miracles-Ebook-40

Chapter 18

Frightened to Fearless

Jenna’s Family

Members of the United States Navy Explosive Ordinance Disposal (EOD) Teams don’t scare easily. It could be said that deep-sea divers work well under pressure, with one hundred meters underwater being more than ten atmospheres of the shore surface. Given this, special operations scuba divers can rightfully boast of being able to handle ten times more pressure than landlubbers. If that isn’t enough for sailor bragging rights, add being a petite woman.

Words like gutsy, amazing, and heroic surface to describe Kathy, one of only a handful of the first female EOD officers in the United States Navy — to be exact, one of five women out of one hundred eighty officers altogether back at that time.

Growing up mostly barefoot as an aqua-beach girl in the Florida Keys, she was equally comfortable in the water with her flippers as on land with her flip-flop island lifestyle. Most people escape to the barrier islands to put the farthest distance possible between themselves and their past, or to ideally disappear far away from others. If they tried to get any farther away, they would fall into the ocean. That is why Kathy’s mom moved them to the southern tip of Florida — to get away from a past so as to start a future anew. As a baby, Kathy and her brother spent several months in the care of trustworthy nuns while her mother relocated and stabilized their transition to southern Florida. The kindly sisters agreed to keep the two children, but only if they were first baptized Catholic. Kathy’s mother consented, but never found a reason afterward to mention God or religion or their Baptism while they were growing up. As a result, when Kathy enlisted as a sailor and went to a Boot Camp Sunday event, she was the only sailor who did not know the words of the Lord’s Prayer.

A naval career seemed like a natural progression to guarantee Kathy would always be near the sea or shore. Military life is disciplined and structured, something she craved as the child of a mother drawn to a carefree life. Yet, despite the stability of military life, there is no controlling, commanding, or taming an ocean or life at sea. This dichotomous combination made Kathy an ideal sailor and future naval officer, eventually rising to the rank of commander. She was a balanced combination of orderly consistency while being equally comfortable with the unpredictable chaos of a wild ocean.

Navy life proved to be a perfect blend of her adult stability superimposed upon her bohemian childhood. Kathy knew how to roll with whatever waves swelled before her, big or small. Anyone who meets Kathy is surprised by how genuinely sweet and gentle she is, while they are trying to imagine her expertly assembling or dismantling a ticking explosive device under pressure, underwater. In addition to her unique skills and impressive accomplishments, Kathy is surprisingly modest, sincerely humble, and noticeably attractive.

Years into her career, Kathy was stationed on the coast of Texas and happened to attend a social activity for singles with impressive music at a non-denominational church service. Rob had agreed to play guitar for his brother in the church band at the service. Rob and Kathy met at dinner afterward and started dating soon after. They were both in their late thirties, knew what they wanted in a marriage partner, and were quickly convinced they had found it in each other. Rob also played guitar in his parish for the Youth Mass and invited Kathy to come to hear him play. Kathy was drawn to the holiness of Mass through Rob and his faith. The couple met with Rob’s parish priest when they became more serious about their future together. Kathy spoke to her mother and was surprised to learn she had been baptized — and Catholic !

It was no wonder Rob was captivated with Kathy when they met and that she was equally charmed by him. The sea-loving gal was instantly smitten with the land-loving geologist, just in the way opposites attract. As much as Rob loved the earth’s vast surfaces and studying them, Kathy equally loved the oceans and exploring them. They were as perfect a complement to each other as surf and turf. Rob was a genius guitar guy. He brought his music with him to college as he was picking up a degree in geology in between guitar-playing gigs, his true first passion. Geologists are scientists and exacting diagnosticians who study landslides, earthquakes, floods, and volcanic activity. Most people who study the solid, liquid, and gaseous matters that comprise our planet are also strong environmentalists with keen insight into the delicate balance and dependency that exists between the earth and its creatures. It is much the same with ocean goers having an inherent love for the water and all its inhabitants. Rob was perfect for Kathy. He was a cool guitar-playing guy, grounded in land and science — with the added attraction of having deep faith.

Rob and Kathy agreed it was worth the wait to have found each other as they were nearing forty. They took their vows seriously and felt extremely blessed. They were both happy to be married in the Church. Not too long after leaving the altar, they were thrilled to discover Kathy was pregnant! During their first full year as husband and wife, Kathy entered RCIA (the study in preparation to enter the Catholic Church), growing in her new faith and pregnancy. She received the Sacraments of Holy Communion and Confirmation at the Easter Vigil Mass, holding their precious one-week-old daughter, Jenna.

Three weeks later, Kathy surprisingly spent her first Mother’s Day in the hospital with her beautiful baby girl. At just four weeks old, Jenna suffered a serious seizure. For anyone who has never witnessed someone having a seizure, it is frightening to stand by, helpless to intervene, initially not realizing what is happening. Not interrupting a seizure is said to be the best thing to do. Laying the person down or turning them on their side is the safest response. For a parent of a small baby, a seizure is terrifying, especially the very first time it happens.

Jenna was eventually diagnosed with idiopathic seizures. Rob and Kathy explored how best to care for Jenna. They decided Rob would become a full-time dad. In just a few short years, Kathy would conclude her twentieth year of military service and retire with the rank of commander. Kathy could then stay home with Jenna and Rob could return to his geology career. Before completing her twenty-year hitch, Kathy was deployed to Kabul, Afghanistan. Just the mention of that painful six-month separation from her precious first-born baby — not her deployment to a Middle East war zone — still makes Kathy cry. Military service members are admirably tough, indestructible, and duty-ready — but they are also loving family members. Their sacrifices are incomprehensible and unimaginable to most people who will never experience prolonged separations from family and home, much less lengthy separations under extremely dangerous conditions, harsh circumstances, or family turmoil. Because they had strong faith and a marriage built upon a mature and stable foundation, Kathy and Rob endured their heavy-hearted half year apart as well as any young family with a special needs child.

Jenna’s seizures increased relentlessly in frequency and intensity. Upon returning from her overseas assignment, the Navy compassionately transferred Kathy back to Texas to be near family support. The parish priest adamantly said, “Go to Lourdes!” Then, Rob’s father, a faithful Catholic, insisted they take little Jenna to Lourdes, offering to send the family as his gift to them. They went through the process of obtaining a baby passport for Jenna. They were packed and ready to fly out when Jenna aspirated fluid into her lungs. She was admitted to the hospital the night before departure, which abruptly canceled their trip to the holy shrine in France. Then, two more babies quickly arrived, and both seizure-free. Their family was complete as Kathy’s military service concluded. She retired honorably after two decades of distinguished service. As a growing family with a special needs child, travel seemed too difficult, and Lourdes was eventually deemed impossible. Jenna developed multiple health issues. She was diagnosed as a quadriplegic, non-verbal juvenile with a severe seizure disorder. Jenna would never walk or talk or grow up the way her brother and sister would. She required twenty-four-hour care. It took both Rob and Kathy together to care for all three children most days. Nurses were brought in to help with Jenna, but sometimes constantly rotating nurses seemed like more work than help. Around that time, private duty and home care nurses were becoming increasingly difficult to find.

Life became stressful, and often downright scary. Jenna was susceptible to choking and could easily aspirate at any time. Bedridden, she was at risk of pneumonia. Jenna’s condition was scarier than underwater demolitions for her commander mom or environmentally catastrophic oil spills for her geologist dad. Being the parent of a child like Jenna is not for fainthearted parents. Their Faith had taught Rob and Kathy intellectually that God never allows us to encounter more than we can handle, but it sure seemed like He knew exactly where the boundary was, and they often felt as though they were dangling over that steep edge. Sometimes something can make us so fearful that we can forget we are always in God’s loving, providential hands. “Jesus, I trust in You!” may become the only soothing mantra for frantic, frazzled parents. We may also need our Heavenly Mother to gently build up our weakening trust through her perfect trust.

Years went by for Rob and Kathy and the kids. Everyday life became as routine as could be expected. Most days were filled with medical appointments for Jenna in heroic attempts to gain some type of control over her ongoing seizures. The two younger children entered Catholic school, and the family struggled on, but Lourdes was always in the back of their minds. They never stopped longing to go to the Grotto, and then a pilgrimage became a possibility.

Our Lady of Lourdes Hospitality North American Volunteers provides Special Needs Pilgrimages with medical volunteers offering supportive care. Extensive planning and every detail are carefully and thoughtfully addressed. Even so, the idea of traveling anywhere, especially out of the country, was daunting for Kathy and Rob. Encouraged by relatives, friends, and priests, the entire family left home for France.

The medical volunteers were amazingly helpful. The care they provided for Jenna was a loving gift of their skills and talents. This allowed Rob and Kathy to spend time with each other and with each of their children. The week unfolded in a series of graces that strengthened and renewed their faith. They both became confident in God’s Will for each of them and for all of their children, especially Jenna. The family returned home relaxed and peaceful, filled with joy.

The family felt called to return to the Grotto again the following year, despite the financial sacrifice. They were not disappointed. Our Lady always has a grace reserved for everyone who comes to visit her in the Grotto, each and every time. Sure enough, the graces continued to flow for the parents and children for a second time. They were a different family returning to Lourdes — less stressed, happier, quicker to laugh and faster to smile. It wasn’t easier to be the parents of a child with intense care needs; it was simply easier to carry their responsibilities lightened by the power of faith and the grace of God. There will always be risks for a child with intense special needs like Jenna, and good parents like Rob and Kathy will always be justifiably concerned. After Lourdes, it was as if their constant fear became more manageable as their faith strengthened into a profound trust of being totally in God’s Hands.

Entrusting Jenna to the Mother of God and her Son in the Grotto removed some of the excessive weight of responsibility by replacing weary worry with trustful surrender. Faith and fear are the flip sides of the same coin. At any moment, only one face of the coin can be seen from a single angle. Focusing on faith and flipping fear behind sounds noble, like a quick or easy spin, but it is hard for parents of young children facing dangerous or severe life-threatening health issues. In Lourdes, Rob and Kathy were able to focus on their faith, diminishing their fears. They were changed as parents and transformed as a family.

A year after returning home, Rob, Kathy, and their three children were invited to speak in Rome at the Vatican to share their experiences in Lourdes. Their son was asked by a Vatican official what had happened to their family in Lourdes. He was articulate for his age, explaining, “Before Lourdes, my parents were always afraid for Jenna, and that made me and my little sister scared. After Lourdes, my parents weren’t afraid all the time, so me and my sister are not so scared anymore. We are happy at home; we laugh and do more things and go more places.” When his little sister was asked what the difference was before and after Lourdes, she said, “We have lots of statues of Mary everywhere in our house now; little and big statues, all kinds of statues of Our Lady of Lourdes — she is everywhere! And we are happy because she is with us always and watches over us all the time, so we don’t have to worry anymore.” The Monsignor in the Vatican understood and reminded them that they are always safe with Mary.

Fear not, for I am with you, be not dismayed, for I am your God; I will strengthen you, I will help you, I will uphold you with my victorious right hand. (Isa. 41:10)

Fearless Family of Faith

The family continues to grow in faith. Rob has returned to work as a geologist while Kathy cares for their children at home. Now, Kathy chairs the Special Needs Advisory Committee for Our Lady of Lourdes Hospitality North American Volunteers. The family plans to return to Lourdes.
