Chapter 19

Mother Knows Best

Colleen and Maris

Colleen was a social worker because she loved helping people. It was not just what she did — it was who she was. After she was diagnosed with breast cancer, Colleen continued helping others by listening to the worries and woes of the women she met while undergoing her own treatment. She officially retired with her cancer metastasis diagnosis, but Colleen continued her unofficial job of helping others because she was always doing something good for someone else. Colleen had been sober for thirty years, and this personal struggle had helped to hone her perseverance and steely determination, as well as her compassion for those who faced addictive challenges. When balanced with her considerate kindness, her gutsy resolve made Colleen the ideal advocate and advisor to many. Countless people benefited from her professional and personal care.

Colleen was increasingly aware of her spiritual well-being as her body surrendered to her advancing disease. One day at church, a friend offered to go to Lourdes with her. Both Louise and Colleen believed our Heavenly Mother knew best what Colleen needed, because moms most often do. Colleen discussed the international travel and spiritual journey with her oncologist and was encouraged to go by her physicians. She was ready for June until her next round of test results arrived. Her doctor said June was no longer possible and advised her to move up her trip to May. Then, more scans revealed that May was not feasible. In March, her physician said April was her only option. Time was running out for a pilgrimage to France and for Colleen.

Most people who are ill or disabled think they cannot travel internationally or domestically, especially if they use a ventilator or oxygen, receive infusion therapy or kidney dialysis, or are immobile. Following the guidelines given by primary care providers, dedicated volunteer medical teams of Lourdes Volunteers create detailed plans to ensure what is needed will be readily available to be lovingly provided during transatlantic travel and during the stay in France. Colleen wanted to go to Lourdes despite her implanted port and medications that greatly weakened her. A mini pilgrimage was already in quick planning, specially created for a large family with two children recently diagnosed with a debilitating incurable disease. This type of pilgrimage can never be arranged far in advance. Our Lady reveals whom she wishes to bring together in the Grotto — just in time. Graces arrive as the plans are coming together. Colleen was able to join an impromptu April Easter family pilgrimage, in God’s time.

Her volunteer nurse was a recently retired full bird colonel, who was still adjusting to walking into a room where everyone no longer jumped to attention as she entered. The Mother of God was gently weaving together a few special ladies to meet for an exceptional week through an extraordinary little sister, her sweet brother, and their amazing family. Our Lady of Lourdes has proven time and again to be expertly adept at this type of gracious, intricate handiwork. She threads people together on her Lourdes travel manifest to receive the blessings awaiting them in Lourdes in a delicate fabric of sweet grace.

Traveling to Lourdes is tiring, even for a person who is healthy. Domestic flights to the pilgrimage gateway city for international departure preload fatigue long before the draining transatlantic flight. For the seriously ill, this travel is beyond exhausting. And, as Colleen said with a smile, “Cancer seizes and squeezes every ounce of energy out of you!” Drifting off to sleep on the last flight in the smaller aircraft from Paris to Pau (the airport not far from Lourdes), Colleen sensed the same exhaustion in a woman nearby. They were both weak and smiled knowingly at each other whenever their eyes met.

Passengers in wheelchairs are the last to deplane, as it is easier for people in transfer chairs to exit an empty aircraft. As they waited for wheelchair assistance, Colleen felt badly for the other woman. Colleen was accompanied by a friend, a nurse, and a pilgrimage leader dedicated to helping just her. The other woman was by herself, alone. The two women were immediately linked by a heightened spiritual awareness perceived only by those who have intimately known serious illness and physical suffering firsthand. Colleen reverted into her helping professional self as the two women waited together. She introduced herself, and Maris responded with a musical accent. Although exhausted, she was happy to be stopping by Lourdes on her way home to Nigeria. Both were going to the Grotto for the first time and, left unsaid but understood, likely the only time. The conversation faded as Maris did, weak from her long travels and treatments. Colleen immediately enlisted the pilgrimage leader to assist Maris. “Would it be possible to give my new friend a lift to Lourdes?” Colleen’s smile was impossible to refuse.

Once seated inside the private transport to Lourdes, Maris produced a scrap of paper with the address where she had been referred to stay. It was so far from the Sanctuary that it was obvious to the pilgrimage leader that Maris should not go or stay there. Besides, she didn’t have a wheelchair. The chair she had used was loaned to Lourdes Volunteers for her by the airport and was for use only in the terminal. How was she going to find a wheelchair in France and make her way to the Grotto from so far away, by herself without speaking French? If the family with young children was uncomfortable having someone unknown join the small group on at the last minute, Lourdes Volunteers would arrange to room Maris on another floor with a volunteer dedicated exclusively to caring for her. The young parents could see that Maris needed help. It was as if God and Our Lady had placed Maris in their midst. They quickly prayed to discern that she should join the pilgrimage. It was similar to the way Colleen and Louise signed on, just with no advance warning or planning.

Maris was invited to join the small pilgrimage family. She was humbled by the offer and, frankly, too tired to resist. The Advance Volunteer Team waiting in Lourdes was asked to prepare a room for Maris while the little family pilgrimage made its way from the airport to the Accueil Notre-Dame. The Advance Team volunteers knew that Lourdes Volunteers did not have the funds to pay for Maris. There was no time to make appeals for donations and no time to hesitate to help her. Lourdes Volunteers trusted in God’s providential care. If God had sent someone in need, He would surely send all that was needed, too.

After Maris rested, the volunteer nurse made a Supportive Care Plan with her. Maris revealed that she was the oldest known adult survivor of childhood sickle cell anemia. Maris’s mother had instilled indebtedness to the Blessed Mother in her as thanksgiving for her miraculous, unexpected survival. Maris had received two bone marrow transplants at a prestigious American hospital. She had just left the United States after another lengthy hospitalization. She felt Lourdes was a good place to go on her way home. The volunteers pulled together all Maris would need for her time in Lourdes. She was without luggage or complaint. Maris was too tired to eat, so she slept from her arrival through the next morning. Maris believed all she needed was provided for her by the grace of God, through His Mother’s intercession, and she was moved to tears by His generosity and her graciousness that she was receiving through them in Lourdes.

Colleen wanted to rest, but she could not keep anything down. She asked to be hydrated through her port, refusing hospitalization. She insisted she had not traveled that far to stay in a French hospital room. She was in a hospital bed in the same building overlooking the Grotto where Pope John Paul II (since canonized St. John Paul II) stayed when he was in Lourdes in his wheelchair. She said if it was good enough for him, it was all good for her. Colleen was relieved to finally be there, just annoyed to be throwing up the only French food she had been able to eat thus far. Finally, she was able to sleep after she received an infusion of IV fluids.

Every pilgrimage is special, made unique by each person and moment encountered. This specific pilgrimage turned out to be extraordinarily special. Maris was delightful, and Colleen was thrilled to be surrounded by wonderful, faithful people who blended into a little holy family pilgrimage. After praying in the Grotto, with the encouragement of her pilgrimage nurse, Colleen decided she should prepare her soul in this holy place to be ready to meet God whenever He called her, which would be soon according to her doctors back home. She had not been to Confession in years. She began to prepare a list of the sins of her life, but deemed it insufficient to take into the confessional. She said she needed to be fully wiped clean before her godly encounter. A thorough list was required, but preparing such a detailed litany became overwhelming. A priest would be helpful. But Colleen had to first get to a priest.

Colleen confided in her assigned pilgrimage nurse. She had every justification for delaying Confession from morning until afternoon, or until the next day, or the day after, until she would likely run out of time. Colleen painstakingly prepared and redid her never-ending imperfect lists, but could not complete the task. There was a kind Franciscan friar on the pilgrimage, but Colleen wanted an anonymous “stop and drop” Confession to offload the sins of her entire life. She was worried she was dying trying. Her faithful nurse insisted on helping Colleen to the Chapel of Reconciliation, where numerous “unknown” priests were ready and waiting to absolve contrite souls that would never see them again. In the Sanctuary, priests speaking different languages are stacked in a saintly lineup row for an anonymous holy hit-and-run sacramental encounter. It was ideal — if she could only get there.

Colleen had come to Lourdes fatigued and weary. She wanted the consolation that everyone she loved would be all right when she was no longer in this world to care for them. Colleen wanted to put everything in order, especially her eternal soul. St. Bernadette went to Confession after going to the Grotto, and so did Colleen. First, she went to the baths, where she emerged refreshed and peaceful. She was then ready for the Sacrament of Reconciliation, which is what she needed eternally in the other world rather than a physical cure limited to this brief lifetime. Colleen was given the gift of peace, a true peace that penetrates radiated bones and failing bodies, filling up the soul to eternal capacity.

The pilgrimage seemed to go quickly, like a dream. Maris had to depart one day earlier than Colleen and the rest of the group. Maris said she was loved beyond measure, just like St. Bernadette was quoted as saying. She was at first sad to be spending Easter without her family at home, but now she was joyful to have spent Easter with her newfound family in Lourdes.

While checking in at the airport, the leader again noticed that Maris had only a passport and a very small carry-on bag. Strangely, she had no checked luggage. Her flights were a series of individual one-way tickets. Obviously, something had gone wrong with her travel reservation and her baggage before she had arrived in Lourdes.

Maris had explained what had happened. She was exhausted from her American hospitalization and had fallen asleep in the Paris airport while waiting to check her bags on a different airline for her return flight to Africa. She missed her flight. When she woke up, all her belongings were gone — absolutely everything! Her carry-on, briefcase with her phone, laptop, medical records, and bank card were nowhere in sight — no luggage. The only things she had left were what she was wearing, her passport, medications, and a little cash that were in the small bag she had fallen asleep clutching as a makeshift pillow under her travel-weary head. Her other hand held her rosary beads, which were her only remaining possession. Airport security doubted her belongings would be recovered. Besides, she did not want to accuse anyone of stealing her things or send someone to jail in France. With charity and forgiveness, Maris said they must have needed her things more than she did.

The airline personnel were moved with compassion and tried to make the best of her unfortunately bad situation. All flights to Abuja were sold out for days. It was the busy Easter holiday week with European school breaks. Without access to more money at home, it would be a challenge for her to stay in Paris. The agent behind the counter tried to help:

“Do you know anyone in France?” the agent asked Maris.

“Only Our Lady of Lourdes. I have never been there, but I know her!” Maris said.

“We have just one seat left on the flight to Pau. Would you like to go to Lourdes?” the agent asked.

Maris instantly considered whether she should stay in Paris and go to her embassy to try to find or replace her belongings, or if she should accept the kind agent’s offer. The round-trip ticket was exactly the only amount of cash she had left.

“You must decide quickly — that plane is about to board!” the agent added, hastily. “I think you should take this ticket and go,” the agent whispered, smiling, and urging Maris.

In faith, Maris decided to get on a plane without money, luggage, or a plan.

“Yes! I will wait in holy Lourdes for my return to Nigeria,” Maris answered.

The agent found a place on the internet for her to stay and wrote down the address on a slip of paper. Maris had flown tearfully to the South of France, trusting her Heavenly Mother would be waiting for her and anticipating her arrival, like a good mom who knows her every child’s needs. Then, Maris met her new friend, Colleen, and was treated like a princess in Lourdes, which she called the home of her Heavenly Mother, the Queen of Heaven. Maris was wheeled, fed, loved, and cared for like a holy mother would do for her beloved daughter who came home for a surprise visit. Maris cried, saying, “Lourdes truly is Heaven on Earth!”

Colleen returned home with the sins of her life forgiven and at peace. The Mother of God really did know what she needed. Louise remained her true friend. Colleen was hospitalized the day after returning home and died two weeks later, her soul having been well prepared in Lourdes. The colonel nurse who helped her get to Confession grew to like entering a room without everyone standing at attention for her. She humbly returned home and followed the example of St. Bernadette by making the Sign of the Cross thoughtfully and reverently as an act of faith and a genuine prayer in and of itself. The little family with two sick children received a special grace, especially the parents. The volunteers said they felt as though they received more than they gave, especially the university students. Maris experienced love without measure, as St. Bernadette would say.

After returning home, Maris sent a gift to cover the pilgrimage expenses for someone like her who might unexpectedly show up anywhere along the way on a Lourdes pilgrimage, to ensure the graces continually flow. And they do.

I was sick and you took care of me. (Matt. 25:36, NRSVCE)

Smiling Lourdes Ladies

Colleen and Maris became close friends in a short time — in God’s time — to be forever Lourdes sisters, sisters in Christ. Colleen was grateful for her holy experience at Pau and in Lourdes. Maris remained a member of Lourdes Volunteers.
Q83443-Everyday_Miracles-Ebook-42
Q83443-Everyday_Miracles-Ebook-43

A

Chapter 20

Holy Hurricane Rescue

Mister Earl

The Ninth Ward has been known as the epicenter of the African American community in New Orleans, Louisiana (LA), affectionately called NOLA, for half a century. Over time, the neighborhood flourished and then slowly descended into an intense and continuous struggle against poverty and crime. Still, the community maintained the familiarity of a tight-knit neighborhood. Despite the statistics supporting its bad reputation, longtime residents bristle against outside criticism of their ward.

Five miles away, in the nearby Seventh Ward, is a highly regarded all-boys school everybody calls St. Aug. St. Augustine Preparatory School was founded in 1951 to provide a classic education to local young black men of Catholic families. The school was known to be strict and scholarly from its very beginnings. Students and graduates were revered as intelligent, disciplined, and well-rounded young gentlemen, each respectfully called Mister. Headed toward impressive educations and professional successes, ninety percent of St. Aug graduates were accepted to the most elite private higher education opportunities in the United States.

In 1969, after the tragic assassination of Martin Luther King, Jr., prestigious Harvard University actively recruited and accepted one hundred black students into the incoming class, including a few bright young men from St. Aug. Ivy League schools soon discovered that the brilliant New Orleans students were ideal collegiate candidates who excelled academically.

Earl was lured away from Louisiana to study at Harvard as one in one hundred of over one thousand two hundred incoming freshmen arriving on the esteemed Cambridge-near-Boston campus. Earl earned his degree in English. He was and still is a lover of words, writing, literature, books, and education. Later in life, he chose to pursue his other life’s passion and began a career as a chef. More than thirty years later, Earl moved back to the Ninth Ward to teach English at his alma mater, St. Aug, and to be closer to his mother and extended family.

Earl was a humble man of faith and a big guy who could easily be mistaken for a professional football player. His personality is as sweetly gentle as he is physically large in stature. As a middle-aged southern gentleman, he noticed his knees bothered him. Standing for long hours at a stove, or in a classroom, or walking long distances became difficult. In June of 2005, Earl made a pilgrimage to Lourdes with his St. Aug school classmate and his wife. The three of them were at Harvard together and had remained good friends.

Walking on the pilgrimage became so painful that by the end of the week, Earl reluctantly ended up riding in a voiture (the blue rickshaw-like vehicles unique to Lourdes used to transport the sick and disabled around the vast one-hundred-twenty-five-acre Sanctuary Domain). One week in Lourdes and one day in a voiture earned Earl a glimpse of the humility and struggles St. Bernadette lived in Lourdes and, afterward, as a religious sister in Nevers. He was especially drawn to St. Bernadette. It could have been her illiteracy, poverty, or simplicity that drew him in. It was possibly the French connection between Lourdes and New Orleans, or simply a grace gifted to Earl. For whatever reason or cause, he latched onto petite St. Bernadette as his patroness. Big Earl and little St. Bernadette were spiritually bonded in the Grotto. Harvard graduate Earl had found his unlikely role model in a holy new Heavenly friend, uneducated St. Bernadette Soubirous.

Earl carried around a good-sized book touted to be the most accurate writings of St. Bernadette and the Lourdes apparitions. Originally an eight-volume set available only in French, a sizeable condensed single volume was eventually published and translated into English. Bernadette Speaks, written by the late Fr. Père René Laurentin, is revered as the most widely respected work on St. Bernadette and Lourdes. Earl was continually reading the hefty book and never seemed to tire of learning more about his newfound spiritual sister. He returned home from France filled with St. Bernadette and Lourdes.

Earl, along with everybody else, had no idea that Hurricane Katrina was about to drastically change his world and the Ninth Ward forever.

A neighbor asked Earl to keep an eye on his elderly mother for the weekend. It was just in case she needed something, and he was told the independent senior seldom did. It was nearing the end of the month, and Earl’s neighbor would only be gone for a few days. Her son felt better knowing someone was close to his “mam.” That’s truly how the Ninth Ward traditionally was within. Longtime neighborly residents had a willingness to lend a helping hand to each other. Although Earl did not know his neighbor, he agreed to check in on his mother, in keeping with ward loyalty and centuries of cultural respect for the elders in the NOLA African American community.

Practically speaking, there are more warnings for hurricanes than there are actual hurricanes coming ashore. After all, hurricanes are erratic, which is what makes them so unpredictable and scary. Many hurricanes divert course away from the projected trajectory, hitting some other soon-to-be-sorry location, while some storms lose intensity transitioning from water to land — and sometimes, just as unexpectedly, blow off the predicted course or return out to sea. When a major hurricane does make landfall, mayhem frequently wreaks havoc that is unimaginable to anyone who has never seen the aftermath of such a hurricane before. Earl decided to leave when the evacuation warnings initially flashed across his television screen. He knew better than to sit this one out, he said.

A man of his word, Earl went to help his neighbor’s mother, just as promised. He explained that they must leave together. She was a stoic lady and refused to budge from her apartment. She had lived through storms and warnings, having survived some of the deadliest hurricanes that had ravaged her beloved hometown of New Orleans. Her childhood was filled with stories of the 1915 hurricane, which destroyed twenty-five thousand buildings a few years before she was born. She knew all about the treacherous NOLA dames — she survived Audrey, Betsy, and Camille (a Category 5)! The feisty lady flat-out refused to leave her lifetime of belongings and family memories. She didn’t have much, but she treasured what she had. There was no convincing her to leave her apartment. A man of integrity, Earl would not leave without her, despite the apparent impending danger to both of them.

Hurricanes have always plagued the Ninth Ward since long before it was settled by the French in the early nineteenth century. The ward is located on a lower indentation of former bayou land that dips six feet below sea level. The area is bordered by a system of engineered levees and is easily subject to flood damage when water gets trapped within it, without an outlet for relief, which is why locals refer to New Orleans as “The Bowl.” Hurricane surges can reach as high as thirty feet, easily breaching the ten-foot-high protective levees. Once submerged underwater, the pumps drown. Once failed, the pumps are unable to relieve the destructive swelling, rendered useless to dispel water. Altogether, this was a dangerous mess waiting to happen. The Ninth Ward was about to endure the worst-of-the-worst in a perfect-storm scenario.

Hurricane Katrina crossed the southeastern Florida coast as a Category 1 storm and intensified in the warm waters of the Gulf of Mexico. Evacuation warnings were blasted on the internet across the coastal states days before Katrina was feared to return inland. Most of the people in the Ninth Ward got their information only from television news — not the internet. The warnings were widely broadcast on television screens, but only a mere forty-eight hours before the deadly disaster was about to strike.

Despite knowing its hurricane history, New Orleans’ Ninth Ward remained the preferred home for neighborhood families, surviving and persevering through storms and struggles for generations. Residents can refuse to evacuate for many reasons: fear, lack of fear, no place to go, or no way to get out. Looting had also become a common misfortune in recent years. Earl’s neighbor was worried that if she did not stay home to remove storm water immediately after the influx subsided, mold would quickly spread in the August heat to ruin everything the hurricane did not destroy. “No sir, Mister,” she said kindly — she was not leaving. Earl’s elder was not rude or stubborn, just firmly adamant, convinced she could withstand any oncoming troubles. Earl was eerily unsettled. He knew they should leave. He could have gotten her out with emergency services and rescuers a few times, but she downright refused. She was a dignified woman of strong character. She defiantly stood firm on her increasingly wet ground.

The water was rising fast. Katrina was reported as downgraded from Category 5 to Category 4, but the winds were still slamming inland at one hundred thirty-five miles per hour. The opportunities to get his neighbor out were decreasing as the effects of the storm intensified within the ward. The National Guard and Coast Guard were searching for stranded residents or any holdout stragglers who had risked staying behind. What was likely her last hope of safe evacuation finally came to kindly coerce her out in time. It took Earl’s firm urging and the heroic rescuers’ frantic insistence to ultimately convince her she had to leave. It took the National Guard to budge her out of her apartment! The Coast Guard carried her out, but there was no room for Earl. He assured them he could make his way out on his own.

The rescuers no sooner left when the levees broke loose.

Earl looked around his apartment one last time. He knew he could only take what he could carry. He eyed everything he owned, realizing he would never see his home the way it was, if ever again. What should he take? He decided to grab one thing, his favorite book, Bernadette Speaks. He left his apartment, wading in dark water over three feet deep. The water was rising rapidly, fueled by erratically vicious winds. Weighing over three hundred pounds and towering over six feet tall, Earl was a mighty-sized man. Hurricane Katrina brought enormous swells of water, powered by great gusts of wind that dwarfed his tall stature. Earl thought of St. Bernadette arriving for the first time in the Grotto. She heard a great gust of wind, but everything was still. It was not so for Earl — Katrina was howling with gusto and whipping winds. Earl was carried wherever the gale forces took him; fighting against the force proved impossible. Houses were knocked off their foundations, swiftly floating or tossed about, slamming into equally sized, massive building debris. Cars drifted down what looked like canals, the stormwater concealing the roads underneath. Heartbreakingly, dead bodies floated by just past Earl in the surges of the swift current. They were almost within his reach. He felt helpless and was unable to quite reach them, even though he knew he couldn’t do anything for them anyway.

The water was murky with a combined mixture of Gulf saltwater and Mississippi River sludge that made it impossible to see anything beneath the surface. Dangerous broken objects lurked sharply under the swirling swells. The powerful and massive wind-propelled water displaced landmarks and strangely mismatched objects, forging them together, sprinkling debris like dangerous glitter that landed precariously where it did not fit or belong. Everything was out of place. Moving down what used to be his familiar boulevard was arduous for Earl. The water rose above his waist, reaching almost chest high. Only his height made it possible to continue toward higher ground or a hoped-for chance encounter with more rescuers searching for survivors. Earl held his St. Bernadette book above his head in an effort to save it and to hopefully flag down someone to help him. Then the levees broke. A wall of water with deadly force knocked him so hard that his glasses flew off. Earl knew he would die.

Earl cannot see a thing without his glasses. If he could not see where he was going or the visible dangers scattered everywhere around him, there was no way he would get out alive. He was reaching underwater, groping with his hand, hoping his glasses were somehow floating near enough for him to retrieve them. He knew it was an impossible and unrealistic search. Earl needed a miracle. He shouted out loud, screaming into the storm:

“Bernadette, you lost your home, and I now lost mine. You were poor, and I am now poor. But, Bernadette, you could see! I need my glasses! HELP ME, BERNADETTE! PLEASE!”

In the swirling storm, a petite hand gently grasped his bigger hand to press his glasses into his palm, firmly closing his fingers into a protective fist, securing his glasses tightly within his grip. Crying, Earl put on his glasses. He knew at that moment he would survive. Earl could not see anything. He could not see St. Bernadette under the water or after he put on his glasses. But he knew it was her. St. Bernadette Soubirous heard him. She found him and his glasses so that he could see his way out of a stormy deathtrap. Years later, a priest told Earl it was an angel who came to save him. Earl simply knows he cried out in faith and a Heavenly intercessor responded.

It was exhausting to move in the increasingly high water. With his vision restored, Earl was steady and able to persevere in the after-flow of receiving a personal miracle. Earl found rescuers, or they found him. They waited on a high bridge to eventually be evacuated to Texas. His neighbor was safely relocated to Texas and reunited with her son. As expected, Earl lost everything in his apartment and the apartment itself, except the treasured book, drenched and ruined — and his glasses.

Tragically, 1,833 people died as a result of Hurricane Katrina.

Hurricane Katrina is the costliest hurricane in the history of the United States, with an estimated $160 billion in damage along the Gulf Coast. It destroyed eight hundred fifty thousand homes, three hundred fifty thousand vehicles, and two thousand four hundred sea vessels. Hurricane Katrina and the damaging aftermath are how most people outside of New Orleans came to know the Ninth Ward. Sadly, catastrophic flooding, problematic evacuations, and the death toll brought national attention to the totally destroyed neighborhood.

Earl contacted Lourdes Volunteers in September from the Houston shelter where he had been evacuated, insisting he had to volunteer in Lourdes, and as soon as possible. He said he had to give back. He needed to honor his beloved St. Bernadette, who rescued him in the hurricane. Although he had lost all he had, he said he had much to be thankful for. Despite Hurricane Rita dumping more water to further damage New Orleans just one month later, Earl flew to France. Earl offered himself for one week in the Sanctuary with St. Joseph Service as a brancardier — stretcher bearer. The Hospitalité Notre-Dame de Lourdes was delighted to have him in service.

More than one million people were displaced by Katrina. Earl lived in government shelters and housing that had been arranged for victims made homeless by Katrina, including the entire population of the Ninth Ward. For months, there was no phone service, electricity, or drinkable water in the ward. As Earl’s elder neighbor feared, mold destroyed much of what the water did not. It was almost a year before Earl and others were able to return to New Orleans.

Earl silently weeps whenever he tries to tell anyone about Katrina and the miraculous saving grace of his glasses through his beloved St. Bernadette. A well-spoken yet soft-spoken lover of eloquent prose and poetic literature, Earl tears up, struggling to find words to relay the profound experience of receiving a lifesaving intercession in his moment of need. When asked about the credibility of the apparitions at Lourdes, St. Bernadette said: “Je ne suis pas chargée de vous le faire croire mais de vous le dire” (I am not made to make you believe, but to tell you). Like St. Bernadette, it is not Earl’s responsibility to make anyone believe him, just to tell them about his saintly best friend, St. Bernadette.

I will forget no one. (St. Bernadette Soubirous)

Katrina Rescuer Rescued

Earl still has a special devotion to St. Bernadette and Our Lady of Lourdes. He remains in the Ninth Ward. His former neighbor died a few years after Katrina. Earl plans to return to Lourdes again and to visit Nevers in thanksgiving and to honor his friend in need and deed, St. Bernadette of the Grotto.
Q83443-Everyday_Miracles-Ebook-44
Q83443-Everyday_Miracles-Ebook-45

A

Chapter 21

Pandemic Prayers

Nurse Lindsey

A microscopic plague shockingly changed the entire world in 2020. Almost everyone was caught off guard or was ill prepared for what would unfold across the globe. The reality is that we should always be ready to meet God, for we never know the day or hour (see Matt. 24:36). Yet, until our individual time is up, most of us (including the older and supposedly wiser among us) will always think we have just a little more time . . . until we don’t.

A worldwide pandemic brought this truth to the forefront for many, both faithful and non-believers. Hospital nurses were especially impacted by this raw reality. Probably more than any other strata of life or profession, nurses had the frightening front row seats in the rapidly unfolding human shock drama at the beginning of the second decade of the shiny new millennium. As a registered nurse, Lindsey was unknowingly positioned front row, center.

Several years before the pandemic, Lindsey embarked on her new professional career. After college graduation, she found that shift work and nursing, in combination with family and social commitments, wrongly took precedence over her Sunday obligation to attend Mass. Before she knew it, an entire decade snuck by without the Sacraments. A graduate of a Benedictine college, Lindsey knew how to “talk Catholic” when required, such as in a circle of older church ladies or in meetings at a Catholic hospital. But it was little more than talk. She began to wonder if she was really still a Catholic. She was surely still somehow a Christian, but not practicing her religion. She had a stable existence, but was feeling mediocre — or whatever describes wondering about the purpose of life. Was there more to life than just routine daily living? Turning thirty, Lindsey was feeling somewhat alone. She liked to travel, but that could also easily lack purpose. She providentially came upon a website about nursing volunteer service missions. Lindsey called Lourdes Volunteers, dutifully “talked Catholic” in response to the questions asked, and signed up for a feel-good service week in Europe. In her mind, the South of France sounded like the ideal place to do something meaningful while technically not on a French vacation.

During her first week serving with Lourdes Volunteers, Lindsey had a life-changing experience after the baths with a return to Confession and Holy Communion. Suddenly, reconciled to God through the grace of the Sacraments, her life purpose was spiritually corrected to align both in how to live in this world and where to aspire to go in the other world. She also found a strong spiritual link in a maternal bond with the Mother of God. Reflecting on her childhood, she realized how much she needed this motherly relationship, especially as an adult. Lindsey returned home to her life and to nursing forever changed.

Following a faith-driven path anchored in the Sacraments was profoundly grace-filled and rich in virtue. Although she had been living a somewhat contented existence, Lindsey never realized that her life had been so mundane and tedious. It had been lacking in authentic joy and the vibrant passion of her rediscovered Catholic Faith. Surprisingly, after Lourdes, Lindsey was burning on fire to live her Faith — like a fanatical Evangelical, she chuckled to herself! She wanted everyone who had been away from the Church to come back and find what she had discovered at Lourdes. Lindsey could hardly contain herself from sharing her newfound treasure of faith. She felt the best way to do this was to return to Lourdes in thanksgiving and again serve as a nurse, helping others who could not travel without medical supportive care.

Her next assignment as a Lourdes Volunteer was serious, demanding, and challenging. This was not because of the severely ill special needs pilgrim with a ventilator she was assigned to, but because the woman’s husband had been away from Confession and Communion for over fifty years. Lindsey understood how easy it was for someone to have a decade slip by and gradually drift away from the Sacraments. She imagined how easily additional decades could swiftly fall by the wayside. She pondered that the longer a person stayed away, the harder it would be for them to find their way back. Lindsey was discreet yet helpful in guiding the good man and dedicated husband back into the confessional — the answer to the silent prayers of his ventilator-dependent wife!

That particular pilgrimage was filled with many graces, as were her first and multiple subsequent pilgrimages. Each time she traveled again with Lourdes Volunteers, the Hand of God was revealed in the healing of a soul or a return to faith; every time a Heavenly gift was given through the gentle and tender intercession of the Mother of God at the Grotto. Lindsey was enjoying the purpose, joy, and peace she had discovered in her life. Then, suddenly a virus struck her city, her country, and the entire planet, hitting especially close to home in her hospital.

In a large metropolitan city in the northeastern United States, COVID-19 hit hard and fast with a deadly learning curve. Precautionary measures were quickly implemented, including restricting visitors. Mandatory personal protective equipment (PPE) was required in the hospital. When Lindsey suited up for work, she thought she looked like a cross between an astronaut and a zombie from a scary Hollywood B movie. As a nurse, she worried what her patients would think or fear seeing only her in her gear, while unable to see their loved ones and friends or have any usual face-to-face contact. The more patients were swabbed, the more positive results were reported. The spread of the deadly virus was fast and ferocious. Lindsey was soon involved in the care of more than one hundred COVID-positive patients. More than thirty would die while in her care — seven dying in one shift alone. It didn’t take long for the hospital employees to become infected, despite all the strict precautionary measures. They were short-staffed in every area, from housekeeping to laundry to medical and administrative personnel. Brave, courageous, and persevering, Lindsey and her co-workers amazingly cared for both their patients and each other. As a nurse living her Faith, Lindsey knew how significant the Sacraments were to the sick and dying — both in this world and going to the other world. Lindsey knew she had to find a priest willing to suit up and come in to anoint the Catholics and bless any others who would be comforted by a spiritual, religious presence.

Franciscans have been caring for the sick in every plague since their founding in the twelfth century. One healthy young priest-friar in the archdiocese had volunteered to isolate himself from his brother priests for the length of the pandemic. His only contact would be with the sick that he would go out to anoint. He learned how to safely put on protective gear and how to enter and exit isolation, as well as how to live quarantined. The heroic young friar essentially took on the austere spirituality of a lone hermit. In his habit, underneath foreboding protective equipment, Father looked like the friar from Mars. Meeting Lindsey, he discovered a nurse with faith — and Lourdes Water! They became a holy dynamic duo in the hospital when Father was allowed to enter. Any patient identified as Catholic or asking for a priest, either for themselves or through their family’s request, was visited by Father. Many of the infected dying patients were older, and it was heartbreaking for them and their families to be separated without physical closeness or family contact. The spiritual care that Father provided was a real comfort to them. He suited up individually for each patient, for each separate room visit — again and again. As an added holy bonus, Lindsey and her co-workers were able to go to Confession and receive Holy Communion. She wondered if these would be her last Sacraments. Nobody expected what was happening or could imagine what might transpire next.

Then, unexpectedly, Father was no longer allowed in the hospital due to the health department efforts to slow the death toll. It was an unfortunate situation for the patients, hospital staff, and priests. Suddenly, the medical team took on the added duties of spiritual care in lieu of a priest or family members. Some patients were with the limited staff for only hours, some for days. Modern technology and social media allowed visual screen contact, but the human interaction of the physicians, nurses, and aides was crucial to the isolated patients and a comfort to the separated family members.

From her service with Lourdes Volunteers, Lindsey knew she had to serve the sick inflicted with the virus in a way that extended beyond the limitations of the physical medical care she could provide during a pandemic — spiritual care was also needed for the eternal souls of her patients. Lindsey was living the Message of Lourdes she had learned in France in her American hospital. Lourdes Volunteers helped fill her holy arsenal to fight the virus with rosaries, scapulars, prayer cards, Miraculous Medals, and, of course, the liquid grace of Lourdes Water. Room by room, Lindsey offered the small holy gifts to Catholic patients and any patient who wanted a little holy treasure, inspiration, prayer, or all of the above. Each person was thrilled to receive a holy gift or to have Lindsey quietly pray with or for them — something that did not happen before the pandemic. People who never mentioned or thought about God were suddenly asking for prayers. Lindsey realized there was truth in the First World War expression, “There are no atheists in a fox hole.” Ironically, that saying and war came about a century before, around the time of the Spanish flu, the last deadly worldwide pandemic.

As death entered into the global conversation, holy thoughts and prayer of any kind became acceptable, often welcomed in the sterile clinical settings previously lacking spirituality. Catholic or non-denominational, formal or spontaneous, silent or spoken aloud, the various prayers not only comforted the sick, but also their families. They were greatly consoled to know their loved one was not alone, but instead prayed for in their dying moments with the nurse present to them.

One woman arrived to the ward unconscious. Finding rosary beads in her personal belongings, Lindsey wrapped them securely into the hand of her silent patient, praying the prayers aloud for her. Sometimes, people are praying for us or for those we love — and we have no idea! This sure knowledge can be a comfort for those who were unable to see or be with their loved ones during the pandemic. Knowing that this one nurse did this can be a comfort to all of us. Surely many more must have done the same without our knowledge.

The hospital staff wasn’t only called upon to provide extra care before their patients’ deaths. While postmortem care was typically performed by funeral home employees in the hospital morgue, the escalating virus exposure risks shifted this responsibility to the hospital staff. That duty was quickly assigned to the nurses. This was something Lindsey had formerly done as a student. Early on, many of the deceased were Catholics who died without the Sacraments. Lindsey remembered the “baptism of desire” and the generosity of God’s Divine Mercy, which supersedes our human limitations. Our omnipotent God was surely present among them in the hospital and everywhere the pandemic was spreading. Lindsey prayed silently as she respectfully cared for the dead. She tenderly wrapped their heads and bodies with sterilized coverings, somewhat as Christ was prepared with His Holy Shroud. Lindsey’s nursing work became her holy work. She cared for each person, living and deceased, as she would care for Jesus Christ in His wheelchair, on His Cross, and in His tomb.

One of the nurses kept a whiteboard at home with the names of the ill patients listed for prayer. Lindsey’s name was soon added to the list along with her patients.

After about a month of extended, grueling shifts, she found herself getting short of breath with the slightest physical exertion. Lindsey tested positive, and her month-long personal battle began with the virus she had been physically and spiritually warring against as a nurse. Splitting headaches, night sweats, extreme fatigue, shortness of breath, and excruciating pain with skin sensitivity ravaged her entire body. For days, she would be in the exact same position for twelve hours or more, motionless and immovable, soaking in sweat. Lindsey received dozens of phone calls and texts, but was too weak to respond — unable to lift her head or have the breath to speak. The spiritual warrior who had prayed with and for so many now had absolutely no energy to pray. She had no physical or mental energy to say rosaries, chaplets, or litanies, the very prayers she had recited so frequently at the bedside of her COVID patients. Her pain and suffering became her prayers, as she offered them for whomever was most in need.

When St. Bernadette was sick in the infirmary, she said, “The bed became my little white chapel. Then it became my cross. Then it became my crucifix when all I could do was lie in it and suffer.” Like Lindsey, Bernadette preferred to be caring for the sick as her prayer, but instead, being sick became her prayer. A nurse practitioner and a trusted friend cared for Lindsey, bringing her medication, food, and comfort. Like her patients, Lindsey was not allowed to see her aging parents, her brother, nieces, nephews, or friends. Day by day, Lindsey slowly improved. After a month of quarantine, isolated alone at home, she recovered and was finally able to return to the hospital to again care for others.

Lindsey is forever changed as a Catholic nurse in a secular world. She believes God doesn’t waste anything and that suffering can help to purify souls. God does not make us suffer. She says in His wisdom, He can allow suffering. Bernadette said, “If the Good God allows it, there’s something good in it for me.” Although the COVID-19 pandemic was the worst experience for her, Lindsey says she learned the best gifts from this life lesson. Grateful for her time in Lourdes, which helped her and ultimately so many others, Lindsey brought the Grotto to the sick when the sick could not go to the Grotto.

For this slight momentary affliction is preparing for us an eternal weight of glory beyond all comparison. (2 Cor. 4:17)

COVID-19 Nurse Lindsey

Lindsey recovered from COVID-19 and returned to work at the facility where she contracted the virus. She returned as a volunteer Special Needs Pilgrimage head nurse to serve the sick and suffering at Lourdes as she did and does at home.
